This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief

PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.

embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.

The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that can be achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Co-operative Societies
benefit some millions of persons —their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor sees not only a fair com-
mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply this communistic principle to
Irrigation
and the CO-OPERATIYE IRRIGATION and MERCANTILE SOCIETY
of .AUSTRALIA, Ltd., have already secured three irrigation colonies in
Victoria, which are partially settled and planted, and are negotiating for the
purchase of a tract of magnificent irrigation country in the West. Nego-
tiations are also pending for a Government concession of Murray land, and
there are now
Colonies
half-populated, awaiting the small Capitalist, the employed ARTISAN, or
the man whose sole CAPITAL is his LABOUR.
HEAD OFFICE OF THE SOCIETY IN AUSTRALIA:
49 QUEEN STREET, MELBOURNE.

A LIVE BODY.
In the N S.W. (nominee) Legislative Council last week the President fell fast asleep in his chair and was approached by two medical members who evidently
thought him in need of assistance.
MEDICO No. 1 : “He is alive ? ”
MEDICO No 2 : “ Yes, alive enough for this dead place.”

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 3, 1891.
A Grave for Two.
The Queensland Coalition Government, like
the other and still more famous Coalition of
Victoria, has fallen upon evil days. So far
it has suffered no material loss, for its
majority is still intact, and its hold upon the
salaries is as firm as it ever was ; but its
political morals, if it ever had any, have been
woefully depreciated, and it has dug a deep
sepulchre for two reputations, both of which
were good in their day, though of them both
hardly a vestige remains. It is one of the
worst features of Coalitions in general, that
they usually furnish a fathomless grave for
the public character of everybody concerned.
The one enduring principle of Party Govern-
ment is the principle of contradiction, and
its one unfailing axiom is that the other side
is a liar of the worst order, and that no act
which it ever committed or contemplated
could, by any possibility, be other than
nefarious. Therefore, it naturally follows
that the leaders on each side are, by their
own and each other’s showing, the boss liars
of the House, and the individuals mainly
responsible for the evil practices of their
respective factions ; and when these two
leaders coalesce they thereby both go back on
their life-long assertion that the other man is
a person with whom no gentleman can asso-
ciate without being infected by the foulness
of his degraded intellect. Consequently the
nation, which formerly believed in at least
one of them, is convinced that neither is
of any account; and when they peacefully
swap the principles for which each had once
professed his willingness to perish, the only
reasonable assumption is that neither ever
possessed any principles that were sufficiently
fixed to be worth mentioning. And if, instead
of effecting a compromise in the usual manner,
the one patriot accepts the whole of his
antagonist’s creed and gives up his own, the
result is just the same : the first patriot, by
swallowing the programme which he had
always previously denounced, thereby writes
himself up as a fraud and a public delusion ;
and the second is also a delusion, else he
would lift up his voice in protest when the
principles which he has always endeavoured
to keep clean are swallowed by the person
whose foulness and loathsomeness had filled
him with so much abhorrence. The priest
who sees a dog consuming the sacred
elements, and yet makes no protest, is a
priest of too b*“ an order to command
respect.
* * *
In the old and comparatively honest days
of Queensland politics, when Griffith was
Avont to denounce M‘Tlavkaith as a thief
avlio had looted the Treasury and carried off
the proceeds, and Avhen MTlwraith was
accustomed to treat Griffith with pitying
scorn as one part fool and one part raving
impostor, the two political chiefs Avcre
apparently enemies to the death. Their
foud was more bitter than even the average
run of such feuds, and apparently the one
desire of each was to expectorate upon the
other s grave, and insult Ids dishonoured
remains. Yet life goes smoothly enough
with them in their new capacity as friends
and allies, though Griffith has never yet,
so.far as we are permitted to know, with-
drawn his assertion that M ll wraith should
be prosecuted for his dealings with the
public funds, and MTlwraith has said
nothing to explain away the position ho
formerly took up —that Griffith’s inherent
baseness and unreliability made his accusa-
tions not worth refuting. Of the two, how-
ever, Griffith has made by far the
smaller sacrifices. Ho has consented to
associate with the individual whom he
'formerly professed to regard as <r political
leper, and thus he tacitly gives the lie to his
old assertionsconcerninghis opponent’s moral
uncleanness, or else writes himself down as a
person to whom lepers are congenial society;
but otherwise he has lost nothing. His old
policy of Imperial grovel is still in the ascen-
dant. He is still the same alleged Liberal and
muddled Socialist who was wont to yearn
oyer the cause .of the poor, and whose yearn-
ings never did any good to any single human
being save himself. He drops the same tear
over the world’s inhumanity as before, and
writes the same noble sentiments as he used
to do, and treads on everybody—also-as he
used to do—who wants him to sacrifice some-
thing more than sentiment in the interests
of the democracy. In every sense ho is the
leading spirit of the Government—a Govern-
ment of militarism, loyal diivel, and ancient
Toryism, strangely disguised by alleged
longingsafter universal equality~an anomaly
as unaccountable as a republican monarch,
or a Christian hangman, or a gaol-flogger
who runs a society for the spread of humanity
in the intervals of his professional duties,
* # *
And MTlwraith, the strong-willed Re-
publican of old, now acts as the humble
follower and subordinate of his former
opponent, and blunders along with him to
one common grave. To do this he aban-
doned his claims upon the Premiership of
Queensland, and threw away the great politi-
cal following which he had himself organised
and led to victory. The situation was unique;
for a i arty to abandon its leader is by no
means uncommon, but for a successful leader
to abandon his party, his principles, and the
highest situation in the State in one act is an
astonishing conjunction of circumstances,
MTlwraith formed the National organisa-
tion, and then within a few short months cast
it overboard ; he drew up the National pro-
gramme and straightway jumped on it with
both feet. He ejected a Governor, and suc-
cessfully defied the authority of the Crown,
and then wrecked his own cause by joining
the party of Joyalism ; he was the most con-
spicuous Republican of Australia, and when
lie might have been the first President of the
free Australian Commonwealth ho threw
the chance away to be a secondary apostate
in a bitter enemy’s broken-down Cabinet. A
few years ago he was the one great leader in
all this continent who refused to have part
or lot in the infamous Naval Tribute policy,
and who fearlessly exposed that scheme as a
device which would place the country at the
mercy of its own fle^; a few months ago, in
his new capacity as*,*a very small politician,
he swallowed his own utterances, and ac-
cepted the scheme which he had so elo-
quently denounced. Sir Thomas MTlwraith
did not sell himself in the ordinary sense of
the term ; he rather paid for the privilege of
giving himself away. Other men have aban-
doned their principles for a consideration in
money or position; the Nationalist chief
abandoned his victorious following and his
position in one act for no visible considera-
tion at all. Possibly he did it simply because
he didn’t know what to do with the follow-
ing, and felt that he must get rid of it at any
sacrifice. He may simply have started
business as a patriot from motives of expe-
diency, and found that the trade of being
even a successful patriot was too extensive
an undertaking for his own moral capacity,
and that his soul was not large enough for
the position.
# # * v
At all events, there is no shred or vestige
of national principle in the pblicy of the
dreary Coalition It is openly and undis-
guisedly a Government with a narrow and
grovelling policy—a Goyernment .of shifts
and expedients and mean devices, compared
to which even the dull and miserably
commonplace Coalition of Duncan Gillies
seemed almost pure and high-minded.
Queensland, under the Griffith-MTl-
wraith regime, is the one state in all
Australasia whieh travels on cheap-labour
principles, and taxes its impoverished popu-
lation to pay for the importation of foreign
paupers and the illiterate off-scourings of
Southern Europe and Asia. In a time of
universal distress and poverty, it exists
principally for the purpose of flooding the
land with Italian slaves and low-priced
niggers, so that the planter and the mono-
polist may be supplied with labour at
Chinese rates ; and meanwhile the white
toiler must cither starve, or else learn to
live like his imported competitor, on rice
and maccaroni, and become a more econo-
mical beast of burden than oven the sweated
operative of Eastern London. There is a
petrified, soul-blasting disregard of every
principle of humanity about this scheme
which should be sufficient, in itself, to sink
the Coalition into the lowest depth of the
great sea of oblivion. For not only is the
Queenslander ruined by the competition
thus forced upon him, but ho is taxed, in
the midst of his distresses, to pay for
bringing out the cheap competitor who
helps so materially to bring about his ruin ;
and at the same time the foundations are
laid for a new racial difficulty which may
require centuries for its ultimate solution.
And the twin authors of this scheme are the
alleged Liberal who built his reputation
on the abolition of Kanaka labour, and the
a loged patriot who rose to sudden fame on
the cry that Austra'ia should be for tire new
Australian nation, and for no other race or
people under the sun !
* * #
Moreover, Sir Samuel Griffith, who
sympathised so tearfully, in theory, with the
cause of the poor, has emptied the’"Queens-
land Treasury to express that same sympathy
with guns and military demonstrations ; and
now the same Griffith, in his capacity as
an opponent of the nefarious system of
private property in land, proposes to fill the
gap by trading off the public estate at 10s.
an acre to syndicates and land-grabbers and
other monopolists of all sorts and descrip-
tions. And MHI wraith, once about the
ablest financier in Australia, and now a mere
broken-down cash driveller, concurs help-
lessly in this scheme. Beyond this, the
Coalition has only two features in its policy;
to whoop for the interests of Imperialism
and to perpetrate some dark and elaborate
dodge with the electoral system which will
effectually cripple the rising Labour-party at
the ensuing election. It is a strange and
purposeless combination with a past that only
wants to be buried, and a future that is not
worth hoping for. Its leaders are a pair of
shrieking reactionaries ; its one ambition is
the conservation of the sacred interests of
Property ; and its diet is its own eaten and
digested principles ; and yet withal it pre-
serves, from sheer force of habit, some sem-
blance of a yearning after democracy which
may stand it in good stead when it cadges
for the democratic vote at the next dissolu-
tion. It has never done anything for the
democracy, but it has always looked as if it
was going to do something, and this weird,
unfinished appearance may yet do some
service to Samuel Griffith. As for his
colleague, the Coalition is his political grave,
and he and the Coalition will probablV end
together.
* 45 *
The Bulletin, at one time, was a pro-
found believer in Sir Thomas MHlwraith,
as being the best hope of the National Party
in Australia. It believed in him, not neccs-
Stirily from any absolute faith in his sincerity,
for sincerity is an unknown quantity, and
the man who takes even his own 1 0 U, or
discounts his own promissory-note, may be
surprised to find that he himself is of very
little account when he calls on himself and
tries to collect the money. But it believed
in him because he had staked his reputation,
his party,' and his political prospects on the
issue—because he had so much to gain by
adhering to his principles, and so much to
lose by abandoning them. The Bulletin
would trust even Duncan Gillies or Henry
Parkes— probably the two shiftiest of Aus-
tralian politicians—to be absolutely honest,
when there were large profits in honesty, and
dishonesty was a dead loss. But, as the event
showed, it made a mistake in trusting M‘ll-
wraith. His case was absolutely unique ; he
had everything to gain by being sincere, and
he apostatised in a falling market. He was a
man capable of being great, he spent half
his life in rising to be a great man, and then
he apparently grew tired of the situation
and became a very small one as a matter of
preference. He was a man of iron, and he
voluntarily degenerated into a man of fat.
To all outward appearance he bolted to avoid
success, and the contingency was one which
noonecbyld reasonably have anticipated.
The individual who goes back on his prin-
ciples for the sake of gain is sufficiently
familiar ; the individual who does the same
thing as a losing speculation is as rare as
hen’s teeth, and even more unaccountable.
About Gambling.
Making all allowance for the improved
luxuriance of denunciation that the topic of
gambling naturally evokes amongst the noisier
and more intemperate class of pietists, there
still remains a big residuum of ugly fact in con-
nection with the betting industry. It is a
saddening sight to watch the slender earnings
of the shop-boy and the domestic servant
engulfed week by week in the bottomless
pit of totalisators, wild-cat mining companies,
and other complex modifications, heathen and
Christian, of the unsophisticated pitch-penny
or fan-tan. It is deplorable to think of the
number of carefully-brought-up youths who
are led on by the seductive wiles of the
tempter into petty and punishable forms of
dishonesty, and so go to ruin at the
very outset of their career, instead of
growing up to a respected old age, and
rooking the public in a legitimate and
praiseworthy fashion by promoting finance
companies, and taking fixed deposits for three
years at 12 per cent. There is no escaping the
conviction that the modern prevalence of
gambling in its many forms is demoralising. It
discourages and unsettles industry. It is the
source of an inconceivable amount of crime and
suffering. People everywhere arc taught to
despise the old-fashioned process of amassing
a competence by a lifetime of honest, hard work
and thrift, and selfishness and foresight, and to
put all their trust in I lie chance of a lucky
transaction in land or shares, or of drawing the
winner in a big consultation. The industrious
clerk or artisan is apt to become very dissatisfied
with his weekly wage when he sees people
around him win by the turn of a card in a da\
more than 119 could hope to earn by the labour
of years. Perhaps he risks his own hard-won
savings on the chance that fortune may favour
him next time, and so embarks on the perilous
voyage that in all but about one case out of ten
thousand ends in shipwreck.
„ * # , #
Now, our moralist is quite right in declaring
that even from a business standpoint it is mad-
ness to exchange a certainty for that chance. T 0
say nothing about the moral wide of the question
when honesty, industry, and thrift can secure
to a man a competence for life and the certainty
of a restful old age, then do barter away such
realities for a mcry shadowy possibility of be-
coming wealthy at one stroke is the act of
a fool. But, unfortunately for this argument
there is a defect in the statement of the case’
To speak of the choice as being between a cer-
tainty on the one hand and a chance on the
other is a demonstrable fallacy. Honesty, in-
dustry, and thrift—nay, even selfishness— arenot
in themselves sufficient to ensure success in life.
A man possessing all these qualifications in a
high degree may starve, while around.him the
dishonest, the idle, and the improvident are
rising rapidly to wealth and honour. The
average wage-earner, however deserving he
may be, has no very, bright prospect to cheer
him on. The opportunities of lifting himself
out of the ranks of wage-earners into a position
of case and possible leisure arc of rare occur-
rence. As civilisation advances they become
rarer—and the interest legitimately obtainable
upon savings is all the while growing less. His
chances depend upon Ids power of becoming
possessed by some means of a lump
sum of money. With that he may set
himself up in business, he may find
remunerative investments, he may, if the
amount is large enough, bank it, and retire on
the interest. The possibility of acquiring such
a sum of money is the one thing that can
remove the want, or the fear of want, that
hangs like a shadow over his life. Accordingly
some such idea, as a rule, becomes the object of
his life. If he looks round to see by what
means others have succeeded, he soon discovers
that the slow plodding way is by no mean# the
surest way. It is one way, but not an infallible
way. To judge by averages, his chance is not
even a strong one. 80 far as the attainment of
this given object is concerned, he may put down
the odds at about ten-thousand to one that his
life is going to be a failure. The big fortunes
he sees accumulated around him are mostly, in
some way or other, the outcome of successful
gambling—or of unlovely usury, or both.
* # #
To gamble, then, is not necessarily to stake a
modest certainty against a remote chance. It
is chance against chance. We are now taking
the business view (business views rule the
churches and the world, and probably the next
world) of the matter, and disregarding
for a moment the moral side. The well-
to-do men, as a rule, have not become
so by force of lifelong practice of the
steady virtues. They have made their piles
by lucky speculations, by unearned incre-
ments, by the prompt seizure of casual
opportunities, by mercilessly using the power
which circumstance has given them of appro-
priating the produce of other men’s labour,
the work of other men’s brains. Even if they
have stood sternly aloof from sweeps on the
Melbourne Cup, their accumulations "represent
many ventures which were none the less
gambling, though they were not decided 011 the
race-track. These undertakings are beyond the
reach of the poorly-paid wage-slave. The
means of gambling ready to his hand are the
totalisator and the sweep. That he will lose is
only one remove from a dead certainty. Still
there is a chance for him—a poor and remote
chance, but the one chance he has of making
his life something more than a ceaseless, weary
grind. So he risks his coin, and if he loses,
and loses, and loses again, he feels somehow
that he did have a chance. We are not apolo-
gising for the spread of gambling ; we are not
justifying it; we are merely accounting for it.
Gambling is demoralising and debasing. But
so, on the other hand, is extreme poverty ; so is
hopeless, lifelong toil. If the moral reformer
can succeed in abolishing these last, he will not
need any special legislation directed against the
bookmaker or the tote, except such legislation
as is necessary to make these two machines
gamble honestly.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Upheaval of the “ Pauper.”
The New South Wales Assembly has decided
that the cornstalk pauper shall have a vote on
the same basis as any other citizen. It came to
this conclusion with considerable difficulty.
There was the usual hoot of derision from the
Conservative side of the House, and many pre-
sumably humorous members drew fancy pictures
of the pauper constituency of the future —a
constituency old, bald, hard of hearing, partially
paralysed, and with only two sound teeth in
its possession, which would put up a represen-
tative pauper as its member—probably the
oldest, baldest, and most grumpy and paralytic
patriarch in the crowd. There was the old,
old gag about that alleged rule of an alleged
constitution, whereby every individual who
pays taxes is entitled, to a share in the control
of the State, and the individual who doesn’t
pay taxes stands out, and there were various
other inefficient jokes, more or less flat and
ancient. But the old wheezes all fell through.
The Foreign-trade party, in particular, did not
sail in with anything like its usual vigour; it is
run by a pauper who has gone bung for an aggre-
gate of £IOO,OOO or so, and who would himself
be in a benevolent asylum but for public charity
and his neglect to pay 20s. in the £, and conse-
quently it hardly cared to sit too heavily on the
men many of whom have drifted into the work-
house through paying their debts. Moreover the
members who have friends or relatives enjoying
Covernment pensions, or who hope to enjoy pen-
sions of their own at some future date, felt their
position was weak and said little, for the man
with a pension lives in a benevolent asylum of


tjg own, and an exceedingly expensive one
at that; and his right to vote is just the
name as the right of the cheaper pauper who
lives in a public institution. He isn’t bald
r toothless because his share of the national
alms is sufficient to keep him in hair and false
teeth, but beyond that there is no differ-
ence whatever. The barrister with no
particular practice, who is boosted on to
|l ie bench, and gets disabled in a few
mouths, afld is pensioned off, is a fair
sample of the lofty kind of mendicant who
enjoy ß a vote and lives on charity at the same
time", and whose position is never even ques-
tioned. The semi-paralytic in the asylum is
a convenient subject for sensational humour,
but he is no more comic than the semi-paralytic
who is a life-long bore in the Upper House, or
the paralysed capitalist who has thirteen
separate votes, and uses them all in his loco-
motive chair, to help on the Foreign-trade
interest. And in any case the old gibe about
charity is played out. There is no charity
about a benevolent asylum, and every old and
indigent citizen has a right to go there if he
pleases just as he has a right to go to gaol if he
wants to. He paid for the asylum in his better
days; the institution is supported, in a great
measure, by taxation, and every tax-payer has
a vested interest in it, and has a right, under
certain circumstances, to realise on his in-
vestment. His provision for old age is
certainly less dignified than that of the
man who has laid up his money in an
insurance - office, and bought a portentous
annuity, but it is a provision none the less, and
the one man is no more a pauper than the other.
Moreover, the State owes a debt of gratitude to
many of these ancient individuals for another
reason, for many of them have reared sons who
are now in affluent circumstances, and who
have shunted their parents when they had no
further use for them, and employ themselves
in propping up the interests of Capital, and
in writing letters to the daily press about the
danger of giving the old man a vote. And, in
addition to all these considerations, the lie
about taxation and representation going to-
gether is altogether exploded, for the State
taxes women wherever it gets a chance, and
doesn’t give them a vote ; also, it taxes China-
men and lunatics and convicts —none of whom
have votes —when they have any property that
can be got at, and in various other ways the
alleged axiom of the Constitution is knocked
out in one round. Therefore, the principle of
extending the suffrage to ‘ ‘ paupers ” is not so
absurd as it looks, and the humour that has
been wasted on it is a very wooden substance.
The kind of pauper who isn’t entitled to a vote
has got one already, and the other sort may as
well enjoy the privilege too.

Queensland’s Little Invoice.
Premier Griffith has got his Provincial
Parliaments resolutions through the Banana-
land Assembly in their preliminary declaratory
form. How they will fare when embodied in
what Sir Samuel calls ‘ ‘ a concrete form ” —in
a Bill, in fact, the latest idea anent which is
that it ought to be submitted to the country as a
test-cry at the next election—seems doubtful.
That they will ever pass absolute majorities of
both Houses seems most improbable. It they
should be navigated through these dangers, and
also get the finishing touch of the Queen’s
assent, Bananalantl won’t be able to complain
of want of government. There will be South
Queensland with two Houses of Parliament;
there will be Central Queensland, whose
leaders, as yet, favour only one House in its
Provincial Legislature; there will be North
Queensland with two Houses ; and there will
he the Queensland Grand Parliament, repre-
senting the three Provinces with two Houses.
There will be one chief Governor, three Lieu-
tenant« Governors, three sets of Parliamentary
officials, three Supreme Courts with three Chief
Justices, and (say) twelve Supreme Court
Judges, &c., &c. To set down the Parliamen-
tary and governing expenditure alone, it would
probably appear like this :

The Prophet of the Poster.
Last Saturday the great Prophet of the
Century—he of the glaring coat and the
illustrated pants—made his triumphal entry
into Sydney. He was a long, withered-looking
man with a fierce nose—a man who looked one-
half Hebrew money-lender and one-half Yankee
book-canvasser, and his eyes were a little too
close together, and his hatchet face and his
haggard jaws were worn with much friction in
running after the cash. His ears were large,
and seemed to stand up with incessant listen-
ing ; they were the ears of a man who had all
his life been harkening to the footsteps of the
interest as it mounted up on the promissory
note, or the muffled tread of the subscriptions
that were yet afar off. He had long, bony
fingers—the sort of fingers that were built to
pick the missing threepenny-piece out of the
narrow cracks and crevices of humanity ;
and a wide, business-like mouth in which
anybody would feel naturally inclined
to post a letter, and after looking at it
one naturally glanced at the General’s stomach,
expecting to find there an inscription setting
forth that he would be cleared at 10 p.m. And
around him were the thousands of the Salva-
tionists, with their faces that seemed to be cut
out of one pattern —with their monotonous
sameness of expression, one-half silliness, one-
half burning fanaticism—a howling crowd of
unreasoning devotees with the glorified book-
canvasser for their god. It was the apotheosis
of the Gospel of advertising, and the man in
the triumphal ear was the embodiment of the
new Church—the Church of the big brass
trumpet and the screaming poster and the
flaring lithograph—the creed that is one-
quarter Bible and three-quarters ballet and
performing elephant. He rose to greatness
because he was the one prophet of the age
who knew exactly how to run the tabernacle
and the shop in the same contract. If you were
in a hurry you could call in and be saved in a
quarter-of-an-hour, and large parties were
saved by contract. The new seer recognised
the fact that all the old seers had failed because,
though the world might like redemption much,
it liked a circus more, and, therefore, he
gathered in the masses by embodying the circus
and the redemption in one and the same act.
He realised that his great Predecessor was des-
pised and rejected because He didn’t “bill ’’the
Land of Galilee and paste his gigantic poster on
the barren sides of Hermon, and that He died
poor because He allowed another man to carry
the bag; consequently the General keeps his
“ advance-agent ” always going, and hangs on
to the bag himself. Of all the enormous wealth
of the Army every cent is his, and either he or
his heir can sell off everything down to the last
inharmonious cornet at any moment, and burst
up the holy menagerie, and disperse all the
men in blue pants and all the men in
red pants, and then retire on the proceeds.
He is the preacher of religious freedom, and yet
even the Pope is a microscopic despot by com-
parison ; body and soul, the Army is his and
bis alone. The prophetic end of him may
possibly have risen against the hideous wrong
and injustice of this world, but the cheap book-
fiend section of his soul has always kept him
from denouncing them, and thereby making
himself unpopular in high places. When he
took up the cause of the “ submerged tenth ” of
Fiiigland, he did not heave all the combined
strength of the Army against the great land
monopoly, by which the legion of the lost is
created and maintained. It was more comfort-
able, and more flattering to the monopolists, to
hear of a plan by which the obnoxious resi-
duum could be taken away and allowed to
fester in our far-off land, and presently their
cheques began to flow in freely, and the
burnished nose of Booth began to gleam at
aristocratic tables,and his haggard jaws obtained
a place in “good society.” The Prophet of
Galilee took the opposite course, and came to
grief because he denounced the land-grabber of
old Syria instead of dining with him and gather-*
ing in his cheque. The last Prophet of Islam
died fighting a hopeless battle in the desert for
the same reason, whereas, if he had been a man
of more discretion, he would have said nothing
about national freedom, but would have softly
passed the hat round in Alexandria and become
a species of tame cat about the back door of the
Khedive’s palace. But both these prophets
failed, and Booth has succeeded. His triumphal
progress round the globe culminated in Satur-
day’s ovation at Sydney. The programme of
his reception was an able piece of stage manage-
ment, and there was no single hitch anywhere.
Unpremeditated applause was to burst forth
spontaneously at 2.30 p.m. sharp, and it burst
accordingly. The vestals of the cause were
there in force, mounted on the haggard
cab-horse and the tired and miserable steed
out of the ash-cart. There was a gleam
of exultation in the hawk-like eye of the
prophet, and his hatchet face was illumined by
a hungry smile. And then, amid wild and
general enthusiasm, the procession started, and
the great embodiment of the art of getting to
heaven by advertisement went on his way down
the street in glory. It was his day out.
Balmaceda was just dead in far-off Santiago,
and the papers were short of someone to fill
the column that that hard-fighting Chilian
soldier used to occupy, so Booth came in handy.
Drink and Weariness.
'riii', anti-liquor contingent rose the other night
in N.S.W. Assembly, and sailed, as usual, on a
trackless sea of figures and a shoreless waste of
heaving statistics, to prove that drink is a curse
which ought to lie instantly suppressed. In the
course of the debate the leading crank solemnly
and in all good faith told a pathetic story about
a Sydney licensed victuallers’ picnic, where 600
people consumed 14,088 bottlesof grog (being 23|
bottles to each individual, infants included)
but even this steep assertion fell flat; and if
the picnic had drunk 230 jars of sulphuric acid
per head, the statement would have fallen flat
just the same. The Prohibition party is fight-
ing a hopeless battle, for the incurable weari-
ness of the whole earth is against it.
Man does not live by bread and broad-
cloth alone. The earth is tired—very, very
tired, and it drinks to bury its mental exhaus-
tion. It wants to forget itself, and forgetful-
ness is sold in black bottles and not in ginger-
beer bottles; it wants new ideas and new
inspirations, and it finds them in liquor. Some-
times it dies of the liquor, but if it didn’t it
would die far faster of monotony and desolation
and grey solemnity. The craving for change
and excitement is ineradicable, and there is no
excitement in a teetotal meeting, neither is
there any wild, picturesque burst in the plain,
brown-covered tract; and no really joyous or
self-forgetful party ever sat up late and drowned
its anxieties in passing the big, cold-water jug
and the hymn. The unobjectionable oleograph
showing the drunkard’s family dying hori-
zontally in an attic awakes no happy recollec-
tions, and the teetotal gospel is the gospel of the
dull, humourless clod who goes to bed every
night at ten, and never smiles except when he
uses by chance some old, mirthless, disused grin
that was last employedin the middle ages. There-
fore, the teetotaller, especially he who publicly
makes a virtue of his teetotalism, generally
dies young—dies of horrible sameness, and the
old dead-level of existence. Men do perish of
these unrecognised complaints—they are more
deadly than the wildest orgie. The individual
who spends his entire existence in turning a
grindstone, even under the most improved
hygienic conditions, never reaches a good old
age ; he dies of the sight of the grindstone, and
goes to turn an invisible crank made of air in
the shadowy land. The person who is strictly
moral dies of seeing his own morals always
before him ; the man who neither smokes nor
drinks nor gambles nor possesses any other vices,
peters out because his brain is used up by
hard and incessant contemplation of his own
goodness. The men who drink much in the
nineteenth century live longer than the men
who drank a great deal less in the twelfth,
because even the hereditary capitalist of that
era grew tired of loafing in a stone castle with
no knowledge, no literature, no cards to gamble
with, and no reasonable-sized city to paint red.
Therefore the stagnation entered into his soul,
and he fell into a state of premature old age.
His modern prototype may be found among the
sad and sober Marquesas islanders, who can sit
down under a tree and die by scores at the
shortest notice, merely for want of anything
outside their own dead calm of existence to live
for. The epidemic can be stopped, as in
the case of the niggers employed on
Queensland plantations, by a general “drunk.”
The teetotal crank should consider this
circumstance. If he can offer any new joy
to take the place of drink, then drink can go,
but there is no joy in the dull lecture that is
delivered by the flat-footed teacher with the
umbrella, - or in the ghastly tract, or the
cold and soulless pump, or in the lemonade-.
bottle, which is so nerveless and so ashamed
of itself that it refuses to stand up on
its own bottom. Moreover, the advanced
crank also clamours for the abolition of novels
and tobacco and theatres and gambling and
boxing and horse-racing, and of everything
which makes man’s heart beat a little faster, and
when he is finished this world will be a smile-
less land of solid literature, hymns and tea, and
people will have nothing to do but sit round in a
dreary and frog-like way, and croak, which
is a vulgar synonym for die. The case
lies between liquor and horrid weariness, and
while this is so the pump theorist may bang
down statistics through all the long-drawn
cycles of time without any results.

Attorney-General Simpson, of N.S.W., has
distinguished himself a little more than usual
with his Bill for the better protection of
children. Some of its provisions are good;
many of them are old-womanish, grand-
motherly and ridiculous. It proposes, among
other things, that any person, in whose house
an illegitimate child is born, shall be required
to “give notice to that effect.” Supposing the
person in question doesn’t know that the child
is illegitimate, what then ? If the said
“ person ” is not also the mother of the child,
the chances are about equal that she won’t
know anything save what the said mother tells
her, and the chances are about twenty to one
that the woman won’t tell the truth. Then, if
the occupier of the house is the mother of the
child, she is already bound to give notice at
the registrar’s office in the usual way, and
if the one law can’t be enforced who is able to
enforce the other ? The Bill also proposes that
“ notice shall be given ” of the death of any
illegitimate child under the age of five years.
There is another law already in force which
provides that notice shall be given of the death
of any child, illegitimate or otherwise, as well
as of the death of any and every other person,
and if that old law is of no avail, what use will
the new one be ? Australia is suffering from
too many Acts of Parliament already. There
are enough statutes in existence to tackle
almost every vice under the sun, and if nobody
uses them what can be gained by passing a lot
more which will be left unused also ?
The Ballance Government’s Factory Bill
arrived the other day in the Maoriland
Council, and that body of patentates pro-
ceeded to amend it after their accus-
tomed fashion. The object of the Bill in its
original form was to abolish sweating, to re-
strict child labour, and to provide more security
for factory operatives. The old and hog-like
monopolists at once waded in, and reduced the
age at which children could be employed in
factories to 13 years, thereby increasing the
pressure of cheap infant competition instead of
reducing it ; and whereas the Bill enacted that
any place where four people were employed
should be deemed a factory, and come under
the operation of the Act, they altered the
number to six. Consequently, any brutal
sweater employing less than six people is
exempt from supervision, and can boss the
miserable children under his control into an
early grave if he feels that way inclined ; and
the Council’s amendment relieves a good many
hundred small tyrants, who would otherwise
have been carefully looked after, from any
serious control. Its members personally gain
nothing by this move, for they are large, whole-
sale sweaters—not small ones ; they were merely
actuated by that love of brutality and tyranny
which grows up in the soul of nearly everybody
who is trusted with irresponsible authority.
Then the Council of irremovable despots started
to consider Sir George Grey’s Friendly
Societies Bill—a measure designed for the pur-
pose of protecting the poor man who puts his
savings in a financial institution against the
richer individual who ruins that institution—
and cast it out by 20 votes against 5, after
which it adjourned in peace. This bit of
experience shows that the Maoriland Labour-
party was right in demanding the reform of the
Upper House first; and that the New South
Wales Labour-party wastes its time haggling
over Bills which are utterly useless so long as
there is an unreformed Upper House waiting to
provide each liberal enactment with a sepulchre.
At a meeting of the shareholders of the
defunct Australian Mercantile Loan and
Guarantee Co., held at Newcastle, N.S.W., a
motion was unanimously carried censuring those
members of Parliament among whom £BO was
collected for the defence of J. P. Howe, the
member under committal on a charge of having
swindled the company. This shows how un-
civilised at heart the community really is, and
how undemocratic are the democrats who allege
that in raising money for the purpose set
forth, Howe’s Parliamentary colleagues were
“ endeavouring to defeat the ends of justice.”
The Bulletin sees nothing in the £BO sub-
scription save steps to ensure Howe being
properly defended before a jury—should he be
convicted after enjoying an adequate defence all
the greater will be his damnation. The Bul-
letin sympathises with those who have been
the victims of such cold-blooded rascality as
has apparently characterised the operations of
the company, but it execrates, in this case as in
all others, the brutal and revengeful doctrine
that “the ends of justice” mean the handing
over of an accused man naked to those who
demand his blood. In times of panic there is
no justice in the public, and when, as is often
the case, the law joins in the frenzy there is no
justice in the land at all. Such delirium is not
the exclusive property of the poorer classes, for
the great advocate, Aspinall, was hailed as a
social leper and refused admission to the leading
Sydney clubs because he had accepted a brief
to defend at the bar of justice a citizen charged
with having shot a prince, whom boots the local
public were industriously licking. When civili-
sation really rules, all accused persons will be
defended as well as prosecuted at the expense
of the State, and when a man is convicted he
will be compelled, so far as in him lies, to
defray the cost incurred by the State in prose-
cuting him.

Hon. William Davidson Greatorex Fitz-
gerald, titled drunk, who formerly rode a fiery
charger in the Lancers and lately has driven a
Sydney milk-cart, has just got 18 months for
the forgery and uttering of a £5 cheque—his
third conviction (the two former having been
for embezzlement and theft respectively). Hon.
Great Wreck Fitzgerald is a lucky poten-
tate. The ordinary rum-and-milk man who,
with such a record, comes before the Quarter
Sessions for forgery, gets five years’ hard at
least, and sometimes seven or ten years. Please
let us know of any other third-timer who has at
Quarter Sessions got off with 18 months for
forgery ? It is not long since they sent up a
poor wretched Sydney baker’s driver for 18
months (first conviction) for embezzling 13s.
And pray how long is it since a New South
Wales Quarter Sessions Judge sent up a (first
conviction) man for seven years for a £6 Bs.
forgery? And even though Mr. Greatorex
Fitzgerald has only 18 months to serve, we
shall have to watch him hard to make sure that
he doesn’t get out before Xmas.
Various correspondents, some of them well-
meaning and some otherwise, have alluded at
intervals to this paper, in more or less kindly
terms, as “ the organ of the Labour-party.” In
response, The Bulletin hereby repudiates the
situation in the most vigorous and explicit
manner. It is not the organ of the Labour-
party, or of the Protectionist-party, or of the
Freetrade-party, or of any single Freetrade,
Protectionist or Labour member, or of any
other party or individual whatsoever, outside its
own office. It declines the billet for the suffi-
cient reason that every political party known
in history or out of it has gone back on its
principles and sold its supporters sooner or
later, if it lived long enough to do it,
and the present parties, in course of time,
will probably do the same. Therefore,
in case the Freetrade party should turn
Protectionist, and the Protectionist party
should turn Freetrade, and the Labour-party
should one day become K.O.M.G.’s and howl
for Property, The Bulletin (which has never
yet from the day it started been in any
way under the control of any member of
Parliament) is the organ of none of them
—if it were it would either have to turn
its coat along with them, or else take the
trouble of contradicting its past assertions, and
the exertion might make it feel tired. It is the
organ of purely democratic and Protectionist
principles, because, though parties come and
go, the principles stay for ever ; and it has a
him belief in no politician, alive or dead. It
believes in some a shade less than others, and
that is all the difference.
When Mrs. Ann Riley purchased the land
which came to be known as the “ Riley Estate ”
(Sydney), she paid £7OOO for it. In 1835, about
ten years later, Edward Riley, her eldest son,
was incarcerated *in old Sydney gaol for debt—
“ Price Waning” has the documents in the case
in his possession and a rough valuation was then
made of the property, which fixed its worth at
£lOO,OOO. Twenty-two feet frontage of one of the
by-streets of the estate were sold the other day,
at a price which would give a present value for
the original block of a few thousands short of
three million pounds sterling.

“Led Astray,” at Sydney Criterion, was witnessed by a
full circle on Saturday, and received with shrieks of rap.
ture. But it was rather funny to hear a row of obviously
married men all “out for a run ” endorsing, with hands
and hoofs, the high-falutin which Titheradge has to talk
about the way a man should treat his wife! Mrs. Brough
as “ Armande ” (she got a double reception) was a nicely-
dressed V earning Soul, rather incomprehensible in this
day of remorseless feminine common-sense, by the way,
and the robe she looked best in was the cream woollen
teatgown, the train lined with old gold, the front draped
in cream muslin, bordered with gold, of the “bouquet
scene.” Also the eau-de-nil satin and crystal bead
trimming of the ball act was soothing. Hilda Spong—
greatly improved in style, only why does she hold her
chin in a sort of dentist's first pose of the patient ?—looked
fresh and genuinely ingenue all the time—especially in
the simple white frock of the last act; and “Dot” 1
Boucicault, as the good genius of the piece, was got up
ala the President of the N.S.W. Art Society. Inlfront,
the most interesting woman wore crushed strawberry silk
and cream Spanish lace—only she would hide herself
beyond the box curtain.
There were two garden-parties in Sydney last week, and
these cheap and boring forms of polishing off your social
creditors seem to be getting a footing amongst people
who want to save their candles and keep other people off
their carpels. But the most captivating thing about a
Sydney garden-party is that you needn’t have the wear
and tear of it upon your own gravel-you needn’t have
any gravel at nil, in fact-hut just write a little note to
the obliging curator to let your guests raid in the Botanic
with, the polite hope that he won’t object to your boiling
the afternoon billy on the lawn—and there you are. Of
course there are one or two drawbacks to all this cheap
alfresco joy. It isn’t nice to have a hungry sundowner
(not looking so pretty as George Darrell) moaning in' the
vicinity and swallowing every time you swallow—neither
would every woman care to chatter and promenade with
a fringe of pic-nicking families and nurse-girls for
audience—just as if the bon-ton were going through a
matinee performance for the benefit of the rank-and°file.
Besides which if you give your ffites in a public place in a
democratic city like Sydney where the populace bursts
througli simply from principle should you put up a hurdle
in. the middle of the Domain, the chances are that your
gaping audience will insist upon mingling with you and
skirmishing round the biscuit-barrel—just as if it was the
good old prodigal relative multiplied by several.
And this reminds me that the other afternoon a big
man in a leather apron called in at The Bulletin office
wanting to know who authorised the Botanic Garden
officials to warn him off the public gardens lawn during
the progress of the private garden-party aforesaid.

—begins and ends for some people. Under the heading

together with an inky shriek of rapture at the rags worn
by a. Gaiety dancer, now presiding over the domesticities
of a gentleman whose wife divorced him in consequence !
Of such, fortunately, isn’t “society,” but only- the “social
item.”

The second confessional-dodging of a “ Village Priest *
at Sydney Garrick on 20th ult., was a vice-regaTehullition
improved by a lovely managerial bouquet, hut even that
didn’t force a full d.c., from which one disloyally deduces
that V.R. in the amusement column isn’t so potent as it
used to he. The Faithful Few who did turn up, though,
were probably gratified at seeing more of dear Lady
Jersey than they bargained for. The vice-regal gown was
of black silk and lace, cut fore and aft in quite a capital V
with a cluster of cream and crimson flowers on the left
shoulder-strap, diamond and ruby necklace, and diamond
pins in her loosely arranged hair; wrap of scarlet plush
lined with quilted white satin and edged with ruchdd
silk to match. Mrs. Rupert Carington, who accompanied
her, looked pensive in pale pink, fairy-fied with cream
chiffon and a cluster of cream carnations, wrap in Indian
red cloth lined with pink silk. “ Chumley ” and “Girly ”
Goschen squired the party. There was no thrill about the
“Village P.” The actors mostly anticipated their cue 3
and tangled their English, like people who are too tired
Out to dot their i’s. Nethersole once said, “I snow’’for
“ I know ” (a gentle avowal, perhaps, that the Show was a
frost), at which Lady J. was wicked enough to laugh.
All through; the house regarded the tragic play With an
hilarious expression—especially in the cloister scene,
where the holy light is supposed to appear suddenly on
the Bible: As the Abbd led up to it by soliloquy the pea-
green heavenly radiance was being jerked all over the
stage—giving the Abbd green hair occasionally, and
suggesting that an ordinary bull’s-eye lantern can hit the
mark a lot sooner than a stage Providence. The dressing
was neat, but nothing startling. Nethersole, who took it
call gobbing in Cosgrove’s anus, revived that handsome
butterfly gown in grey silk and black-jet that she wore in
the “ warm ” scene of the “Idler.”
Upton what, I wonder, do women base their deep em-
phatic objection to going out by themselves? Women
always say “ I’ll go if you will,” or “I didn’t go—couldn’t
get a soul to go with me,” even when they’re only after
shopping. But the woman who really won't stir out
without what Becky Sharp calls a “sheep-dog” is the
dubious married flirt who hooks a companion to her
girdle and thinks she can chance it to rouge a little
deeper on the strength of her. One society woman—now
divorede—had a dowdy and demure female following cn
her heels up to the very last moment, who must have
been dreadfully in the way occasionally, or else her calm
and guileless face was a masterpiece; Actresses are never
seen without a mate—very ugly and hideously dressed as
a general principle; but always occupying the second
seat in the brougham cab. Indeed; the rule seems to be
that the smarter you are at adding two and two into four
the more need you have of a gooseberry. And yet it isn’t
the rule, either. I know one girl who simply won’t go
down the Block alone, and she isn’t a Very’ prettv girl
(now, I hope she won’t see this). She Cries out for a kin-
dred soul even if she’s only going to buy a reel of cotton.
Manly Wild Flower Show’ of ’9l can’t be counted in as
another big success because it was the most dismal floral
effort the industrious promoters have ever put forth.
Nearly every stall appeared to have been decorated by
the owner standing with her back to it and pitching the
native tulips over her head—when she could get them,
for there was a great scarcity of “ catch ’’ flowers, the
actinotus being especially few. Perhaps the stall one
least longed to throw’ stones at was the M.H.C.’s, with a
model of a yacht and the club houses; one dreary gap
was filled in by a stretch of sand and a contemplative
King Billy about to boil the last part of his nick-name on
a realistic Are. They disguised another hole in the show’
with bottled snakes, stuffed animals, and butterflies,
apparently because there weren’t enough flowers to go
round.
The thing began badly. Someone had seized the Jersey B
as they left their launch and rushed them, nolens volens,
all over the place, delaying the opening from 3.30 to
nearly 4 o’clock. Lots of Sydney people had come down
lunchless to be in time for the speeches, and these poor
starvelings couldn’t get even a sandwich until vice-royalty
had set “ it ” a-going ; so you can imagine the scramble
there was in the fodder-tent; where pretty amateur
waitresses spilt cups of tea, knocked over vases; and left
plates of pastry on the chairs, letting tat ladies sink
heavily on top of tarts and custard-rolls as if the thing
was a harlequinade. The Countess, who nervously set it all
in motion, sported <icrn; barred with yellow and trimmed
with gimp panels and vest, en sidle ; wide hat in black
gauze trimmed within a “tear a handful as you pass”
sort of way with convolvulus, buttercups, flowering grass,
poppies; all shrouded in a white tulle fall. The only other
lady on the dais was the local mayoress in yellow flora
pongde and a yellow bonnet trimmed with polyanthus,
and who presented Lady Jersey with a very elegant
bouquet of native flowers when she had finished the
speech. Then came cheers for the Jerseys, ending with
some for the Queen, but hardly anybody responded.
“ It’s not in nty line,” said one Manly celebrity, defiantly
Jamming on his grey silk belltopper. Sever before were
so many ’toppers seen in Our Village, which emphatically
sported its high hat On the occasion.
Talking about wild flowers, the Sydney Woman’s
Christian Temperance Union, which wants to abolish
everything but the pulpit and the tea-pot, has just very
fittingly decided that it’s emblem shall be a flannel-flower.
Flannel and old women are So inextricably mixed up that
a better symbol could hardly have been hit upon. And,
talking of the Union, the resolutions passed at their last
cackle show how some women love to run the universe
even if they only do it on paper. For instance, Mrs.
Harris (!) is “superintendent for influencing the pul-
pits!!” and Miss Laverty, ditto for “influencing the
press If!" I should just like to see her influencing the
heathen press with ginger beer 1
They are saying just now that you can’t have every-
thing, for if one Great Lady sets a bad example in one way
the next Personage is just as bad a model in another.
For instance, if Lady V. sets the female community
daubing its nose with powder, and its lips with carmine,
Lady Z., who doesn’t interpolate at all facially, puts rouge
out of court, but is responsible for a wide, blushable
snipping-ous of the tops of corsages de soirfe, because
she likes to be—er—cool after dinner.
When they give a ball at a hospital what becomes of the
inmates? Is the rheumatic sufferer carefully deposited
in the tool-shed, and the convalescent with the broken
leg stowed out of harm’s way k» the wash-house so that
the dormitory can be beeswaxed—in the same way as the
drawing-room chairs and tables hi Ordinary households are
carried into the back yard to make room? And doesn’t
the row interfere with the night’s rest of the patient with
brain-fever, and does the band bang loud enough to
smother the shrieks of the sufferer with the ear-ache ?
The story goes that Maud’s mamma, when up the
Mountains, was charmed by the usual gentlemanly
stranger whom she invited to visit her “in Sydney.” In
the interim Maud’s papa blew up, and the gentlemanly
unknown did call—as a bailiff. If this is all true, Sheriff
Cowper’s deputies must be improving vastly in their per-
sonal appearance.
The annual dance given by Professor West to his pupils
at Leigh Hall, on 24th ultimo, was a success, oven if it
did start at the weird period of 4 p.m. at which dread hour
the grown-up girl who came with a short petticoat under
her long white frock, somehow suggested thejnan in the
moon when the light got behind her. The dancing was
tip-top, especially the new dance (the waltz minuet) the
French cotillon and the English minueWtwo sisters in
cream Grecian dresses lined with yellow silk posing in the
latter with Sylvia Greyish grace. Also a random set of
eight little tibs went through an unrehearsed medley,
quickly picking out what dance was playing. Dainty
refreshments were served in the supper room, and the
card of 15 dances concluded at 8 p.m.

Four N.S.W. barristers are in various
stages of bankruptcy proceedings, and ca sa
applications have, during the last month, been
granted against four others. These facts seem to
indicate a temporary lapse on the part of a gentle-
man who is said to “ take care of his own.”
A piece of “ the true Cross ” has arrived
at Bendigo, and it is stated that pilgrimages to
Bendigo are anticipated. They might as well
hold an auction of the wine that was made at
Cana. Native-born and State-school-eduoated
Australia is outside the region of such credulity.
Apropos of the labour troubles at Moonta
arid Wallatoo Mines (S.A.), it may be remarked
that the S.A. miners are worse treated and worse
paid than any others on the Australian continent.
Nearly all the best of the men who once made
these peninsula mining towns prosperous have
long ago migrated to Broken Hill.

important canvas which has fallen into private

One little fact from the case of the (Sydney)
Australian Mutual I. and B. Company (gone
bung)
Mr. Carl Simmat said he saw the manager of the com-
pany soon after payment was suspended, and ascertained
that all the properties were fully mortgaged. In Eliza-
beth-street there was a property which in 1887 was offered
for sale at £39,000, and he found that this now stood as
an asset on the society’s books of £77,000. (Cries of
“ Shame/’) The mortgage thereon was £32,000, whilst
the income was only about 30s. per week, from bill-
posters and other trifles.
The Bulletin takes the liberty of doubt-
ing the authenticity of the “ Diary of Lieutenant
Ralph Clark,” of H.M.S. Sirius —one of the
“hirst Fleet’’—which served as material for a
gossipy article in S.M. Herald of Sept. 20.
Where are the original MSS. ? How has the
Herald contributor satisfied himself that “the
typewritten copy ” is not a copy of screed vamped
from King and Hunter? In the given extracts
from the diary there is not a circumstance related
that is not to be found referred to in works
familiar to every student of the Australian past,
“ The British Government has recognised
the Junta, or Provisional Government of Chili.”
Just so. But yesterday the men who now com-
pose the Junta were scabby rebels, insurrection-
ists, insurgents, revolters, mutineers, traitors,
rioters, anarchists, seditionists, and—er—contu-
macious strikers. To-day, they form a Treaty
Power, whose flag must be saluted courteously.
Success justifies every rebellion, every crime even
—everything. A year ago, the working meri of
N.S, Wales were, in the eyes of the dominant
classes, so much trash and so much howling and
brutal discontent that Gatling guns and fixed
bayonets and knock-kneed special constables were
required to skim off their fermentation ; now, the
same dominant classes vie with each other as to
who shall bow lowest to their new-found idol, the
working man. And that is just why the Illawarra
working man should stick like wax to Nicholson
and Lysaght, and particularly toNicholson, at this
Saturday’s election.
David Christie Murray writes concerning
TnE Bulletin in the Contemporary Review for
August, 1801 :
The Bulletin’ is very capably Written and
edited. The brightest Australian verse and
the best Australian stories find their way into
its columns. Its illustrations are sometimes
brilliant, though the high standard is not
always maintained. And having thus spoken
an honest mind in its favour, I leave myself
at liberty to say that it is probably' the
wrongest-headed and most mischievous jour-
nal in the world. People try to treat it as a
neglectable quantity when they disagree with
it. But I have seen as much of the surface of
the country, and as much of its people as
most men, and I have found the pestilent
print everywhere, and everywhere have found
it influential.
The Bulletin is not at present concerned with
its critic’s opinions, but with his statement of
fact: “I HAVE SEEN AS MUCH OE THE
SURFACE OF AUSTRALIA AND AS MUCH
OF ITS PEOPLE AS MOST MEN 7 . I HAVE
FOUND The Bulletin EVERYWHERE.
AND EVERYWHERE HAVE FOUND IT
INFLUENTIAL.” Let people remember the
fact, known to every man who travels—that THE
BULLETIN is the ONLY paper which has a
thorough Australasian circulation. The inter-
provincial circulation of any other Australian
weekly is but a shadow of that of The Bulletin.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Go’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
EPITHALAMIUM.
Let the bride blush—it is fit.
Slave in the market-place, sold
To the clinking and chiming of gold,
While all know the manner of it—
Let the bride blush when she meets
Bold, painted Sin on the streets.
And now, after this little thing of my own, believe me
to remain,
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

In “The Sundowner,” now being nlavnd
at Sydney Garrick, George Darrell starts, aphar
ently for the express purpose of outraging L,
bability, and adheres faithfully to the
throughout. The giddy but faithful young w f f “
of Ins hero, Major “ Ned’Mllversleigh, “ tllf S ,
downer,” having fainted, is carried off bv
husband’s false friend (Robert Inman) and lomidl
oil the settee into his arms, comatose but kicking
while the husband,*suffering from a spear-wonim
inflicted by the Zulus, spins round like a liuinmi
top and falls flatly, very flatly, with the top 0 f
skull to the footlights, a mountain of broadcloth
topped with a snow-white waistcoat. In the next
act he looms up largely, just out of a lunatio
asylum, though from his garb one might suppose
he had just stepped down from the window of a
waxworks. Arrived on the station of an idiotic
squatter and his impossible wife (Jenny Watt
Tanner) he induces them, aided and abetted bv a
tract-delivering incubus (Polly Emery), a badly
drawn dude (H. Diver), and the simulacrum' c f' a
young Australian (Arthur Greenaway), to s‘and
in a broiling sun for some minutes while
he tells them how he, “ Dare-devil Ned ”
rode and boxed, danced and gambled and
fought for the Queen and the honour of' Hold
Hmgland, and then, arrayed in a bed-curtain
shirt, corduroys and Hessian - boots, rushes
from the peaceful home, whence he would have
been sooled by dogs twenty minutes previously i n
real life, brandishing a bread-knife and an usher’s
wand, and yelling, “Up boys, and at ’em!”
This fraudulent attempt at the impersonation of
an Australian type, however, toon returns ap
parently for his billy, and, recognising his’wife
(Christine Darrell), now a governess, faints
again after a heroic struggle with her
abductor who comes on at this stage, promiscuou -
like. Somehow or other after this “the Sun-
downer” regains his reason and his bank-
balance unexpectedly. Then the noble young
squatter is stabbed by the villain, and the fourth
act, a histrionic horror, is devoted to the re-
conciliation of wife and husband and the un-
masking of the villain, who washes down bread-
pills witli cold tea in sight of the audience and
Walks off handcuffed to die a miserable death by
poison on his way to the dungeon. The play is
also illuminated by the conventional stage Irish-
man, who twinkles at intervals in the persoh of
Hi Shine ; an unearthly and unnecessary larrikin,
harmoniously handled by A. G. Poulton; and a
French maid of the “Bleak House” brand, but
much deteriorated by travel, convulsively wrestled
with by Constance Deorwyn. George Darrell
doesn’t seem to have much faitli in the drama
himself, and usually plays in a humble and apolo-
getic way as though to say, “ Don’t heave a brick
at me, I don’t mean any harm !” As for Christine
Darrell, she has a sweet voice, a nice stage
presence, and plays just about as well as We
expected;

The run of “A Celebrated oase,” at
Sydney Her Majesty’s, has been marked by
a continuous congregation of lonesomeness around
the pay-boxes and in the circle, doom has slid
silently into the stalls and stolen secretly upstairs
into the family circle and gallery. So far, how-
ever, it does not seein to have altogether taken
possession of the stage, where the company still
goes manfully on its uphill track. If Clifton
Alderson were not so stagey ho would prove
acceptable to most people, and Laura Yilliers
might become a positive favourite were her tasks
more apportioned to her abilities. The trouble
will be mitigated on Saturday night by a change
of the place attacked by the dramatic neuralgia,
for Jerome K. Jerome’s “Woodbarrow Farm”
will be produced for the first time in Sydney.

** * *
Folks are getting a bit tired of the bar-
room and race-course jokes with which the “Blase
Roue” is upholstered, and so “Cinder-Ellen” will
be hailed with glee on Monday evening. A
decadence of theatrical taste as evinced in the
growing liking for burlesque and comic opera is
continually taken for granted, nevertheless, we
incline to think that the deterioration has no
foundation in fact. People simply go to theatres
for different reasons nowadays. In early days
they went to stimulate their brains, now they go
to ease them. In burlesque they have discovered a
gorgeous and brainless pastime which thoroughly
amuses them while enabling them to indulge in
that spice of vulgarity which is secretly dear at
heart to even the most straitlaced of Adam’s sons
and Eve’s sisters. Burlesque, especially Gaiety
burlesque, is idiotic and imbecile, but it requires
no translation or explanation, and keeps folks from
yawning, therefore it pleases. It has been re-
marked that Nellie Barren has acquired, oris
attempting to acquire since her American tours,
some twang of American speech and manner.
Indeed, one of her essays in melody is clearly a
plantation walk-around. It’s a mistake, the
humours are entirely different and don’t blend

Auguste Wiegand’s organ recitals continue
, draw more than half-houses in the afternoons
d fairly full houses in the evenings at Sydney
Town Hall. A great crowd, chiefly ladies, sat
entranced through a long programme, classic
Terns included, the other afternoon. The only
dissentient was a baby in the body of the hall,
vho swallowed a programme and at once entered
1 protest. Perhaps the most popular item was
Gounod’s lovely serenade, given with the remark-
able effect of the celestial voice singing the air
well in front with all the sympathy of a human
bein'*, sustained by a muffled chorus of far-away
gera phs. That sort of refining and educating in-
fluence should be given away free occasionally.

** * *
“The Gondoliers” at Adelaide Royal has
proved a source of revenue quite phenomenal,
and with the exception of the Sara Bernhardt
season, during which the prices of admission were
considerably raised, the takings, so far, have
knocked out the record. Treasurer Tallis is
haggard and prematurely aged with counting the
dollars, and Pete Hughes’ smile has been so hard-
worked that it had to be re-soled. The opera
itself has bewitched the Adelaide public—from
the viceroy down to the humble citizen who fills
the position of dog-drowner to the Corporation.
Violet Varley deserves special mention for her
bright, vivacious acting and charming ringing.
The little lady has shown wonderful improvement.
Flora Graupner does all she knows, and Florence
Young has captivated many a heart. Elsie
Cameron warbles like an artist; Charles Ryley is
a pronounced hit; Syd. Deane grows in favour ;
and the old favourites, Elton, Vernon, Bracy and
Co. maintain their reputation. M. Leon Caron
conducts the orchestra in a masterly style. Owing
to the limitation of the season, “ The Gondoliers ”
must give way to “lolanthe” and “Charity
Begum at Home,” which go up as a double bill.
Stacey, surrounded by a creditable little company,
a dog, and a swarm of “little kiddies,” has been
doing satisfactory business at Garner’s Theatre
with “Hans the Boatman” and “My Sweet-
heart.”
** * *
“Merry Wives of Windsoa ” did a good
first week’s business at Melbourne Royal, but
already the roars of public hilarity are relaxing
their vigour, and the crowd in the vestibule grows
stealthily less. People respect the production,
which is bright and solid and correct looking,
more heartily than they enjoy Shakespeare’s farce.
They do their best to accept the Falstaff of this
play as the “immortal creation,” and struggle
desperately to shake with merriment on the mis-
understanding that he is a barrel of personal
humour, instead of a butt for practical jokers.
All the while he is getting himself placed in
ridiculous situations, and lured on to make an
object of himself. This Falstaff is not the
unctuous plump Jack of “Henry the Fourth,” the
droll old soaker, the mediaeval J B. Buckstone,
who sets the tavern table in a roar, but merely a
hot-headed stout person, befooled by all who meet
him and held up to ridicule in a week’s “wash ”
a very dirty wash, too, suggesting that merry
wives never went in largely for Windsor soap.
And how, by the way, a buck-basket full
of linen manages to take in eighteen stone
of human fat without getting any fuller is a con-
juring trick which The Bulletin would like the
deceased Bard to explain. But no matter. “The
Merry Wives ” has many negative virtues in
addition to the positive charm of the scenery and
appointments. In the first place, it is more than
three hundred years old, and a valuable relic to
begin with. It is giddy in a ponderous sort of
way, and quite delightfully free from anything
resembling a stage villain, as tha.t old and popular
article is generally regarded. Seldom do you get
so much scenery without a villain, for even panto-
mimes abound in crime. Master Ford, indeed,
certainly does organise a picnic-party with the
idea of “showing them a monster,” but Ford,
who, en passant, is a deplorable ass, fails in his
fell purpose. The monster lies concealed amongst
a lot of stockings and unmentionables, and he
wouldn’t be really wicked if they fished
him out. Now, if Falstaff were a sur-
reptitious dude with an insinuating moustache
and a sidelong glance, and if the women
fainted when he told his love, instead of burying
him in shirts and chemisettes, then he would be a
reckless profligate and vile seducer of the deepest
dye. The boys would hoot him dreadfully when
he came, pale and languid, before the curtain. It
is a melancholy fact that the world, and theatre
gallery, excuse in an absurd fat man the very same
lack of principle which is so disgusting in the thin
roue. There is much about “ The Merry Wives ”
to set the philosopher pondering in the gloaming,
or cogitating over during the silent watches of the
eight (not that watches ever are silent in the night
when you wind ’em up). So there is in a football-
match or Sunday-school outing. Life seems to be
full up of reflection. The Rignold company are
still doing ample justice to Divine William’s
ancient jest, albeit Falstaff might well baste his
two myrmidons with more realism, and the
treasury ought to equal current liabilities for
another week.

*m % *
“Rigoletto” secured four satisfactory
attendances at the Alexandra last week, but
“ Nonna ” was inclined to be a little off. Each
performance of the former opera has endorsed a
well-established opinion that Madame Simonsen
brought genuine talent over from Italy. Such
people as Melossi, the Cutticas, and Travaglini
act almost as well as they sing, and only need
more expensive surroundings to make their
strength properly appreciated. The performance
of the principals in “Rigoletto” is more than
good. It is first-class, Guidotti made her re-
appearance last Monday in “II Trovatore.” The
same artiste will give her well-rested voice a show
in “Norma,” during the week, and on Saturday
the Cutticas will wrestle with “The Barber.”
** * *
Considering that “musical comedy” at
Melbourne Princess’s seems almost as wicked and
irreverent as fireworks in a Baptist Chapel, the
general result of “ Kindred Souls,” on Saturday
night, was better than it might have been.
“Kindred Souls,” in its literary aspect, is witless
and maddening—a crazy patchwork of old devices
clumsily stitched together. Nevertheless the
people employed to lighten the darkness contrive
to do something extravagantly comic every now
and then, and a performance inherently silly
becomes as amusing as acting can make it. The
songs that some of them put their lips to when
they feel so dispoged, and which ought to be in-
troduced by a chairman with a little hammer and
a few words to the audience, don’t go far towards
condoning the offence. A solitary exception is a
drinking ditty—“ Flow thou purple stream”—
which Snazelle trolls forth in genuine
operatic style. One can’t call any purple
poison to mind, but that’s only a minor
detail. Perhaps the drinks provided by the
management are of a reddish cerulean hue.
Maggie Moore contributes a very poor
music-hall chant, and Gourlay seems to have laid
violent hands upon “ The Miser ” (which poor old
Georgie Leybourne sang of yore), and rung a
gruesome change on the words. Taking the thing
as it stands, with all its sins upon its head,
“Kindred Souls ” is an improvement on the last
Opera House revival, inasmuch as the ladies are
smarter, and Royee takes a part. Snazelle would
be the soul of the affair only it hasn’t got a soul,
and he seemed to make it go furiously at times.
“ Our Flat ” is likely to be wanted very shortly,
all the same.
** * *
Rice’s “ Corsair ” is to re-open the Opera
House on Saturday. The MacMahons announce
that “The Corsair” has been a remarkable success
elsewhere, and they “ rest with perfect confidence
upon the verdict of a Melbourne audience.”
Meanwhile they must be reposing on the non-
existent, which doesn’t alter the fact that “ The
Corsair ” is a wholesale leg-show coming at an
opportune moment.
** * *
Halls, Cycloramas, Waxworks, ete., are
flourishing as usual, but the new Olympia, in
Flinders-street, has closed its doors after a brief
tempestuous season of two hours, or so. A thou-
sand people, mostly bank-clerks and mashers of
middle grade, tried a shillingsworth of Olympia
on Saturday night, under the harmless delusion
that a Grand National Bazaar and Beauty Show
had commenced. It never struck them that
Saturday would be a bad night for getting beau-
ties together, and when they found a dreary
expanse of floor dotted over by eight small stalls, ten
fearful female apparitions and a brass band, the
boys wanted to take up their “bobs” and walk.
Unfortunately, the arrangements for returning
money at the doors were as defective as the beauty-
supply, and nobody came forward to explain that
the loveliest woman engaged had been unable to
come. In point of fact, the defaulting Cleopatras
were tubbing their grandchildren, or getting in
the vegetables for Sunday, and their absence was
unavoidable under the circumstances. They
couldn’t get themselves cleaned in time to roll up
and compete for a prize of £5O, set apart by
generous management for the angel who had
fewest squints and most teeth. A riot naturally
ensued. One by one the eight stalls were over-
turned, and from a wild chaos of nuts, oranges,
tobacco, scent-bottles and beauty, each patron
grabbed what he could. A rumour that one of
the oldest lumps of loveliness, who was too lame
to run away, had been torn to pieces and dis-
tributed as mementoes of the occasion was current
in Melbourne when this notice left.
At Poverty Point.
George Leitch has lately been lying low in various quiet
localities to let his hair grow, and having wandered to
Zeehan (Tas.) he, at latest advices, had organised a syndi-
cate, and was preparing to start a big theatre. This
establishment is to have a huge auditorium, billiard and
refreshment rooms, offices, shops, a commodious stage,
and, if possible, George Leitch himself to open the show ;
and already all the shares are taken up. If Zeehan Con-
tinues to boom it will be a great and noble speculation ;
if the bottom falls out of Zeehan then the grass will grow
on the stage, and goats will ponder among the scenery,
and the local farmer will rise up and grow corn in the
auditorium. For the present, however, everything looks
brilliant, and there are prospects everywhere. Gouty
speculators come along, and curse because they
damage their toes against the valuable specimens
that lie around in the roads. There are boulders
of virgin ore at every corner, and they come
in handy for dogs to rub themselves against, Zeehan
being the only place on the face of the earth where the
lost cur scratches himself with pure silver. The popula-
tion steals most of the macadam off the roads and
secretly pounds it up for the sake of the precious metal
in it, whereby the highways get into a bad condition.
Everybody is crawling under his house, and clawing the
silver out of the foundations, and in very wet weather
landslides of hard cash are frequent, and people have to
keep out of the way lest they should be brained through
specie payment falling on them. Consequently Zeehan
looks a promising place to run a theatre, especially as the
star comedian can fill in his spare time by grubbing the
money out of the foundations. These statements are
supported by the affidavits of all the horse-thieves of the
island.
The fact that Owen Harris joins Myra Kemble to play
the name part in “ Dr. Bill,” and Charlie Shackleton in
“ Jane,” shows that Owen is getting on.
The old Australian favourite, Sir William Don, was
6fc. Tin. high. One of his favourite stage-tricks was to
walk up to a chair as if he didn’t see it and step over it.
Lady de Grey says she expects Frances Savile to create
a greater furore than did Melba. Richter says her
rendering of the prayer from “ Tannhaiiser ” was the
finest he ever heard.
Jacques, who, on the conclusion of his 50 days’ fast,
carried Mesmerist Kennedy, a man of many stones weight,
round the Aquarium stage, remonstrated sharply, before
the mail left, with a visitor who annoyed him by nibbling
biscuits.
When Dominick Murray, the Irish comedian, and
Josephine Fiddes ran away from their Melbourne engage-
ments and got married, the theatres they were playing at,
the Olympic and the Royal, had both to be shut for the
evening.
An apparently Hebraic tailor at Broken Hill (N.S.W.)
advertises that the original Holy Coat has been sent to
his shop for repairs, and somehow' this par got tacked
on to Alf. Dampier’s theatrical notice. Rossibly Dampier
is short of humour, for he writes to the paper repudiating
the unsavoury outrage, and denying that he has any hand
in putting new tails on the sacred garment.
The Simonsens can’t get an opening in Sydney with the
new opera company for some months to come, even if the
company manage to escape the influenza and hold
together. They are due in Adelaide and Brisbane at
earlier dates.
George Rignold advertised that though the part of
Master Ford was subordinate to that of Falstaff, he
trusted it would be considered to add additional strength
to the cast. No part can strengthen or weaken a cast of
characters, anyway. Besides, George’s mixed advt.
would almost seem to imply that he wrote the part him-
self.

Edwards, at St. John’s, Darlinghurst, Sydney, on the 23rd.
The wedding breakfast was served at “ Carlsruhe,” the
