Haddon Chambers’ likeness by Bassano is now in all the
London celebrity-mongers’ windows.
Sheridan’s agent, Vincent West, is in Hobart Hospital
with typhoid. West ought to be careful where nurses are
concerned. Not long ago a constable w'as placed in that
hospital to watch a suspected man. He entered the
establishment a gay, unfettered bachelor, but, falling a
victim to the charms of the nurse who attended the
suspect, he left it “ engaged,” in consequence of which
much tribulation has fallen on that nurse’s relations, and
she was packed off to Sydney straight away.
George Leitch took a cruel rise out of a brother artist
at the N.S.W. Art Society’s smoke concert last Saturday.
He gave a song which purported to be Johnel Sullivan’s
first attempt as an operatic star, said melody also pur-
porting to be Johnel’s own composition. The production
was so like what the slugger would produce, and the way
it was sung was so horribly and fiendishly like the way
the slogger would sing it, that if he had been there he
would have resigned his stage billet on the spot.
More parachute troubles to hand. Viola, the little
Ballarat girl who married Aeronaut “ Van Tassell” Price,
was caught in an upper air whirlwind at Kataming, W.A.
The parachute collapsed, but the lady, landing in the
branches of a dead tree, escaped with a few bruises.
Says New Orleans Picayune of Olive Berkeley, reported
dead at the same time as Harry Edwards: “It is safe to
say that no little woman in the world of no more than 12
years can play Galatea so well as Olive Berkeley played it
last night. Her acting is a revelation in art.” Gush!
Olive is an old-fashioned twelve-year-old.

A. J. Dixon, a Toronto photographer, has walked across
a cable five-eights of an inch thick, 860 ft. long and 260 ft.
high, suspended at Niagara Falls 180 ft. above the Whirl-
pool Rapids The cable was not properly guyed and
swayed terribly.

kind to him in London, that Mrs. Potter is in Paris, and
that “ The Trumpet Call ” is a play which would suit
Australia.
Jeande Reszke is not a natural top-shelf tenor. His
high C‘s occasionally burst small blood-vessels in the
throat.
Carmenoita is about to retire. Fat is the trouble. It
must be plentiful, since American managers are storming
the famous dancer with offers of from 65 to 75 per cent, of
the gross.

to work his own way up.
Adcock, the insolent Adelaide insolvent, got up an
amateur performance, at Melbourne Princess’s, many
years ago—two pieces of his own writing, one a drama of
the “Queer Client,”in which he acted, while the audience
yelled, “ Ambrose Kyte !” and the other was a burlesque
on the “Comedy of Errors," Dick Younge being stage
manager.

ing, in February, at the new Fifth Avenue Theatre, N.Y.
Chris. Shepherd of the Sydney E.T. department, once
organist at Trinity Church, and well-known in musical
circles, passed away in the prime of life, the other day.
He will be much regretted.
According to Pinero, nowadays we can have no real
comedy because strict comedy is an artificial reflection of
manners, and to-day we have no manners, we cannot
dance the minuet, handle a fan or cane, and know not
how to take snuff. Yes, but just look how we can smoke
cigarettes and stuff stick-knobs into our mouths.
Richard Stahl, the American composer, comes to Aus-
tralia after his European tour, for two years, according to
his own statement. He brings “The Merry Monarch,"
“ Princess Petite,” “The Sea King,” and “Said Pasha.”
The latter is said to be the best and brightest of American
comic operas.
About six dramatists, including Haddon P. Chambers,
are writing, so they assert, plays at fabulous prices for
(hard-up) American managers.
Returned Americans say that “ The Idler ” was much
better played at Melbourne Bijou than in either New
York or London.
Clara Morris had occasion to complain to the carpenter
of a way-back American theatre about the general mal-
odorousness of the premises. To which he replied:
“ Don’t see how that can be. We never have no coloured
troupes. The last actors what was here was dogs.”
Seven American performers, Fay Templeton, Lillian
Russell, Carmencita, Marie Jewen, Pauline Hall, Adelaide
Moore and Rosa Cook, are bewailing their adiposity.
In Australia, Maggie Moore, Jenny Watt-Tanner, Kate
Bishop, Blanche Lewis and others should eschew sugary
and starchy foods.
“According to a statement in a Melbourne paper, Teddy
Royce was taken ill with rheumatism at Dunedin during
his last trip to Maoriland. Further, by the same authority,
liis landlady was a Good Samaritan who shed tears over
his sufferings, and resolved to cure him, and in pursuance
of this kindly resolution she rose early one morning, and
filled the bath with hot water. Then she burst into
Royce’s room, and next moment she emerged with a small
and three-parts-naked comedian howling under one arm
and a huge sponge in her hand. After that there were
wild shrieks and execrations from the bath-room, and
sounds like a very small actor being violently bobbed up
and down in very hot water, and kicking frantically in
the process. Apparently he was hanging on with all four
legs to the side of the bath, and the Good Samaritan was
shoving him in by sheer violence. Anyhow, the tumult
died away in course of time and there was a great calm.
And by-and-bye the landlady made a fresh bolt for the
bed-room with a bright-red but convalescent comedian
under her arm, and a towel in her other hand, and as Royce
re-appeared at the theatre that night the treatment was
evidently a success. As this story will roost likely be
denounced as a fabrication we rise up and indignantly
contradict it ourselves before going any further.

“ M.” (Inverell): We admit that District Court Judge
Murray has a faultless record, so far as a man can have
one, and that he, equally with Judge Backhouse, has
strong claims for advancement to the Supreme Court
Bench. There are, however, several D.C. Judges whom
it would be perfectly ludicrous to seat in the Supreme
Court—just as it is perfectly ludicrous to nominate to
that Court the battered, respectable, spineless weaklings
who often get there through political manoeuvring and as
the result of personal canvass. There are now on N.S.W.
S.C. Bench only three, or, at most four, men who, in the
opinion of lawyers and of the intelligent public, are fit to
be there at a 11.... “H.H.” (Sydney): The Bulletin has
said more, and said it more strongly against Booth’s
scheme than any other paper. It is utter bosh to
sav that the big-drum colonists would “strengthen
the democracy.” They are pledged to abject grovelling
obedience to the Army, which accepts its orders
from abroad, and no slave can help democracy.
They have learned to regard £1 per week as riches, and
would worship the boss who gave them 30s. And if they
were any good they would have shaken up the English
democracy long ago, but they didn’t, for there was no
fight in them.

is off to England again for six months. He’s a

tralia naturally induces the question, “ What has

of the polite question asked at dinner of the

draws as his Governor’s salary exactly £4OOll

It is remarked in Melbourne that General
Booth is not a bit like his Army, but still the
Methodist preacher. As different from his fol-
lowers as Disraeli from his.
“ I have actually given the last shilling I
have had to those who have wanted it more than I
myself did ; I have done that many a time,” says
Parkes. Creditors, we presume.
Seymour Lucas, R.A., killed last week in
a Spanish railway collision, is represented in the
Melbourne National Gallery by a very good
picture—“ The Armada in Sight.”
Most of the Free Kirk ministers in the
North of Scotland have decided that Tummy is
past praying for, and now omit his name from the
concluding prayer of their church service.
Tom Garrett is fast declining physically.
111-health hobbles him indoors, and he seldom
leaves his Newtown residence nowadays. Bis
quondam toadies now ignore his ebbing fortunes.
Sir John Hay will shortly retire,
rumour, from the chair of the N.S.W. Legislative
Council. His successor will be either A. H. Jacob,
W. H. Suitor, or John Lackey. The nomination
rests with the Governor.
When “Bobby” Lowe was at Oxford he
was president of the famous Union, and once fined
an obnoxious speaker a pound for disorderly con-
duct. The unruly one paid up—and became after-
wards Archbishop of Canterbury.
When W. S. Gilbert floated his Duke in
“The Gondoliers’’into a joint-stock company he
was, of course, unaware that years previously
Marcus Clarke, in one of his sparkling “Peripa-
tetic Philosopher ” papers, had prettily used the
same notion.
Rear-Admiral Stephenson, C. 8., is to
succeed Rear-Admiral Scott on the Australian
station, according to English authorities who
ought to know. The reason for his promotion
above senior veterans is—he is an Equerry to the
Prince of Wales!
John Lang’s well-known books, which,
under the titles of “ Assigned to his Wife ’ and
“ Botany Bay,” are to be seen in every bookshop,
originally appeared, the first in Fraser's Maga-
zine as “Emily Orford,” and the second in
Household Words.
Charles Dickens always had his eye on the
Australias. In 1849 letters were received in Syd-
ney from him stating his intention to settle in
Australia. In the sixties, again, he cast lingering
looks thusward. But he didn’t come. Only his
sons came ! Alas, and alack !
Mr. N. R. D. Bond, the nominal secretary
of the V.A.T.C., has we regret to hear returned
from a health trip to England—he was a fellow-
passenger with General Booth—in a very bad
way, mentally and physically. His medical
attendants in Melbourne consider the case hope-
less.
Sir Henry Parkes is the bread-winner of
three families. In addition to his young family
by his young wife, he supports an aged sister at
Faulconbridge, on the Blue Mountains, and the
daughters of his youth, several of whom are
widows, in a suburb easily accessible from Hamp-
ton Villa, Balmain.
The phrase, “my intimate personal rela-
tions with William Charles Wentworth,” has
fallen trippingly from Parkes’ tongue twenty
times within the last five years. We have no
reason to believe that Parkes ever spoke to
Wentworth in his life, though he satin the same
Chamber with him for some years.
The ashes of Madame Blavatsky are to be
divided between Madras, London, and New
York. If fat made ashes every town under
heaven might have a sackfull of the ugly old
witch. And if conscious imposture made ashes
you could with the remains of old Mother Blavat-
sky fix up all the cinder-tracks in Christendom.
Judge Windeyer is now regarded as a
probable Chief Justice of N.S.W. when Dnrlev
retires. Windeyer wears the trappings which Sir
Alfred Stephen wore so long as Chief Justice. If
Crick’s Bill reaches the Statute-book, the judicial
“duds” will have to do elsewhere, perhaps in
Yandemonia, which clings to its Stephen-made
laws.
“General” Booth still has the Notts
accent, but he has apparently been chaffed out of
rubbing his hands, like Varley’s reminiscence of
the old butcherly “ Buy ! Buy 1” With all his
experience, he hesitates somewhat in public speech,
and ever and anon catches himself up, repeating
words, after which he will stamp and pass both
hands through his yet flowing locks.
Clement Wragge, Queensland’s weather-
prophet, is an oddity. Some years ago, perhaps
in a fit of absent-mindedness, he took unto himself
a wife. Ihe honeymoon was spent in a trip to
Europe. Wragge was a source of amusement on
the mail-boat. At any and almost every hour of
the night he used to leave his bride to take some
observations; some nights he hardly left the deck.

Hangar, M.L.C., was admitted at Middle Temple
tour years before Salomons, but then he never
practised, although the legal members in the
Council style him their “learned friend.”

When A. G. Taylor was ejected, years
ago, from the N.S.W. Assembly,'he was stone-
walling the military estimates. He had been cn
the qui vivc for some hours before these estimates
were reached ; and in order not to be starved out
of his revenge on his former military masters he
had come provided with a larder. A roll of
Tribune proofs, a box of sardines, a bag of
biscuits, and a soda-water bottle of something,
possibly whisky and water, weie lying by
Taylor’s side when the Sergeant-at-arms arrested
him.

“General” Booth has no feai’s about a
future state. He knows exactly where he means
to go when the Salvation business doesn’t pan out
any longer in this sphere. Addressing his tur-
bulent “ Army ” at Melbourne the other day, he
grafted the parable of Lot’s wife into the thin
stalk of his remarks, and observed that “ The
Angel’s advice to Lot before the burning of Sodom
‘Fly! save your lives!’ was true Salvation
advice. When he got to heaven he felt he must
hunt that Angel out.” Ask him to come and have
a soda-and-inilk. There’s no pride about the
“ General,”
W. A. Guesdon, formerly of Hobart, who
didn’t leave his son, “Darebin ” Guesdon, a sov.
out of his £300,000, was the first to import the
bronze currency to Tasmania. On receiving £5O-
- with that good-natured eccentricity which
always characterised him, he ad vertised that every
man, woman, and child who called at his auction-
rooms would receive a new penny, halfpenny, and
farthing. In two days he had to shut down on
his offer —people were going half-a-dozen times
over. There is a grim and ghastly reason for
those provisions in Guesdon’s will which exclude
Roman Catholic institutions from benefiting by
his enormous charitable bequests—and the reason
isn’t “ sturdy Protestantism ” either.
Ex-Mayor Gatehouse, of Melbourne, died
on 21st ult., aged 65. He was the rowdiest aider-
man of his time, a “rorty” night-hawk of whom
many lurid stories are told. He was secretly held
responsible on one occasion, when a financial dead-
lock embarrassed the Government, for the sudden
appearance of a gorgeous pawnbroker’s sign
(wrenched from its customary shop-front perch
during the night) bestowed mockingly over the
royal coat-of-arms above the Victorian Treasury
portals. On another occasion a couple of score of
pint and quart-pots were prigged from a hotel,
and next morning, it being the Sabbath, the pious
people wending their way to church via Fitzroy
Gardens were delighted to find all the statues in
that picturesque reserve variously adorned with
these bacchanalian vessels—“ ringed ” on to out-
stretched arms or tied to their necks. It was
Gatehouse’s cheerful habit to go home at 2 a.m.
during his year of Mayoralty on a hot-sausage
cart with a shrieking steam-whistle attachment.
He spent more money in liquor for all hands dur-
ing his official term than any two Australian
Mayors ever did before. One incident of Gate-
house’s career is immortal ; It was he who, in his
civil capacity, placed the citizens’ gold’ laurel
crown upon the noble brow of Madame Ristori.
He put it on so lopsidedly that he made the stately
old dame look as if she had just come off a mid-
night excursion on his special saveloy-machine.

publican “Grandfill.”

There is a Ballarat (Vic.) mine which
rejoices in the name of the “Temperance.” The
other day, a “pup” of the same lead had to be
christened, and a number of digger men had
gathered together to negotiate the ceremony
Their fertility of nomenclature was at first it
fault. “Call it the Single Bottle,” suggested
one. “The Local Option,” cried another. “The
Crystal Spring,” gurgled a third. “ Gentlemen ”
shouted the Chairman, with bellowing gravity
“ the claim is next door to the Temperance; there
fore, its name will be ‘ The Consolidated Jimjaim
and D.T. Extended,’ This meeting will now
adjourn for a drink.”
In a N.S.W. cattle district a grazier missed
a pure-bred shorthorn cow. He rode in hot haste
to the homestead of one whose pickle-tub was
likely to be empty. He saw his cow in the stock-
yard amongst beasts belonging to his neighbour
and so overjoyed was he at finding the animal
alive that he went inside for a social glass. On
coming out, he returned to the stockyard. Lo !
the shorthorn cow was missing, and an unidenti-
fiable carcase hung from the slaughtering gallows
He knew it would be folly to complain, so he
returned meekly homewards. But he drove before
him one of his neighbour’s beasts —a fine, red, fat
bullock, with a bell round its neck. Next day
the neighbour visited him and said : “I’ve come
to inquire about a red bullock.” “I shouldn’t
wonder if he’s strayed away with my shorthorn
cow.”_ A pause, and the neighbour spoke thus
with impressive gravity—“ Mind ye, Davie, it’s
no fair o’ ye to keep yon bell.”
One of the tiny blots on the political
reputation of J. G. Francis, once a Premier of
Victoria, and member of McCulloch’s famous
Ministry which broke the back of the Upper
House, and one which he felt deeply in his latest
and most conservative years, was that he had
signed the petition for a revival of transportation
to Tasmania. The sole signatories to that
iniquitous document were landholders, their
merchant - correspondents and dependents, and
the nominees of the official section, and Francis,
then a struggling merchant in Hobart Town’
could not resist the pressure from his sheep-
breeding clients. W. J. T. Clarke, father of
Baronet Clarke, was another signatory, and he
signed as a landholder, oivning 52,000 acres and
leasing 80,000 acres in Tasmania, and owning
40,000 and leasing 62,000 acres in Victoria ! No
wonder old “Alphabetical’’signed in the interests
of ‘ ‘ cheap labour. ” Shepherds were shepherds in
those days, and wire-fences were unknown.
On the 23rd October the first of a series of
great annual sales of Australian wines will beheld
by Messrs. Piggin and Co., of Corowa (N.S.W.),
when 300,000 gallons of the best and purest liquor
in the world will be offered to the nublic. So far,
Australian wine is probably the only unadulterated
drink on earth, and the unfailing security of the
purchaser is that it is so cheap that the elaborate
process of adulteration would be a dead loss.
With the costly alleged vintages of France, Spain,
and Portugal, however, this nefarious mixing pro-
cess does pay, consequently there is about twice
as much of them sold as all the vineyards of
Western Europe can produce. Therefore 50 per
cent, of them may be genuine the other 50 per-
cent. certainly is not. Anybody who wants to
drink cheap and pure wine, instead of expensive
chemicals grown in a city factory or back yard,
should make a note of this and buy the local pro-
duction.
The Sydney Chamber of Commerce yarded
ex-Treasurer M'Millan in a big dreary hall, the
other day, and gave him an illuminated address,
which set forth that the Lord made M‘Millan and
then rested because his work was complete.
There were one or two commercial magnates
present and about a dozen or two small men—men
"'ho owned a one-roomed office apiece, with a
battered table in it, and a fly-blown calendar, and
a directory with a shattered back, and, a seven-
shillings-a-week office-boy, and one dirty window
looking out into a wilderness of broken cart-
wheels and slush. They greeted M'Millan in a
dreary speech that sounded like a spectral dray
with an ungreased wheel going on for ever, and
M‘Millan unrolled his grey-headed platitudes in
response, and cut off a piece, and handed it over.
The following was his peroration:—
There is no social reconstruction that may not be met
by meetings of both parties on a basis of levelling up;
but if there is anything that keeps me in the public life
of this country, and makes me give up leisure, and time,
and family, it is the feeling that in this, as in all com-
munities, there is an element that would destroy the
very Constitution itself—there is an element ripe for
darkness and misrule; but if this community, as I believe
nine-tenths of it is, is true to the great privileges of our
heritage, and true to the Constitution, which is elastic
hut must not be broken, and true to the spirit of the
British Constitution, and true to the instincts of the
British race, this young community will yet evolve the
brighter Britain of the future.
This sort of thing makes us feel very, very old.
Anyhow, we were a young and giddy bachelor
when we started reading M'Millan’s speech, and
it aged us so rapidly that, before we got to the
end of it, our grandson was grown up and had
started a bank, with a capital of £1,000,000 paid-
up, and a reserve-fund of £480,000.
The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs, and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange,
curious, and refreshing drinks known in the
United States can be obtained. Their name is
legion, but among them may be mentioned, cham-
pagne cocktails, gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—
probably the noblest liquor known to man-
imperial punch, Manhattan cocktails, and many
others. All mixed by American bar-keeps.
James A. Walsh, proprietor, late of the Occidental
Hotel, San Francisco.*

Copeland M.P. sighs because N.S.W. has
not as many marriages as the Cabbage Garden.
However, he didn't top-up the latter’s divorces.
And Haynes who trumped his trick by boasting
of the N.S.W. birth-rate, forgot to study the
local illegitimacy statistics.
What is the N.S.W. Treasurer going to do
for money when the £900,000 or so (which is all
that will be available for spending on public
works out of the recent £4,500,000 loan) has been
disbursed ? The time is coming when the fiscal
question must be faced in earnest.
Victorian Government proposes to place
a three-million loan on the London market early
next year. Premier Munro is, however, vexed
that N.S.W. has preliminarily hypnotised the big
tenderers with an alleged 4i-miilions, that really
brings in considerably less than that amount.
• Good old Sir Henry Parkes reproaches the
N.S.W. Assembly with verbosity. Now, the
more men the more talk. Who is the father of
verbosity ? Who gave a million of people a Par-
liament a quarter as numerous as the House of
Commons? Why, good old Sir Henry Parkes.

right to import foreign enemies of the local Work-
man is the logical outcome of Freetrade.

menaced by Chinese who meditate an invasion.
If Australia hears any more of this kind of Im-
perial bogey-raising she will probably proceed at

itching for a war.

The Governors of the Chinese Provinces
assert that the recent anti-European riots “ were
caused by the aggressive attitude of the mission
stations.” The same old story-buccaneers, first;
illicit and over-reaching traders, second; and
Christian missionaries, only as a background of
colouring. Saltpetre, sulphur, and Scripture in
the order named.
The present N.S.W. political situation i s
full of suggestion. The Legislative Council, if it
holds its ground on the Land Bill, will play right
into the hands of the democratic party in the
Assembly. Parkes should be told to “ flood ” the
Council with democratic blood; or to clear out.
As The Bulletin has continually pointed out,
Parkes is playing for a constitutional deadlock.
In two amendments intended to be re-
strictive of the wholesale alienation of land con-
templated by the Queensland Coalition Govern-
ment, Tom ‘Glassey, M.P.* found himself sup-
ported only by—in the first instance, Hoolan and
Salkeld, and in the second, Hoolan solus. He
had previously offered, if backed up by half-a-
dozen others, to stonewall the measure until
■ Doomsday.
Mr. R. B. Wilkinson, M.P. for Balranald,
is reaping the reward of Ids political perfidy in
turning out the last Dibbs Ministry. He has
brought forward two important bills (a railway
and an irrigation bill) in the interests of his
electorate; but the Opposition are deaf to the
claims of Balranald ; and the sooner it dismisses
its scpiatter representative the sooner will its
wants be served.

N.S.W. politician who left as .a legacy to his
family the dishonoured paper of a leading person-
age in the province. For when Sir George \Vigram
Allen was gathered home to his fathers, a pile of
promissor y notes was discovered among his papers.
The executors were pained ; the exalted personage

Recently a Victorian politician called upon
Sir Henry Parkes at "the public buildings. The
Prime Minister was seated at his table studying
a sheet of ..foolscap on which was gummed an
excerpt frthSfiN Sydney D.T. The conversation
drifted to the mangling of the Commonwealth
Bill in the Victorian Assembly. “ I never
read the newspapers,” loftily observed Parkes.
“ Apparently not,” courteously retorted the other
nodding at the extract.
Apropos of the suggestion that Gladstone
or Parnell could be elected to the N.S.W.
Assembly under the new Electoral Bill, it may
not be generally known that Earl Grey, Secretary
of State for the Colonies, was elected in 1848 to
represent the city of Melbourne in the N.S.W.
Parliament, by a large majority, over a Mr. T. E.
V. Foster. The law officers reported that the
election was valid, and would, therefore, have to
stand until Earl Grey should notify his resigna-
tion. The seat was declared vacated in 1850, but
the election was never gazetted. The Duke of
Wellington, Lord Palmerston, Lord Brougham,
Lord John Russell, and Sir Robert Peel were
nominated for the representation of Port Phillip
about the same time.

Committee? Parkes! The Board of Railway
Commissioners ? Parkes ! The Land Court ?
Parkes ! The Civil Service Commission ? Parkes !
Government Asylums Inquiry Commission ?
Parkes ! And all the other boards and commis-
sions? Great Parkes !

It is rather rough on the warlike news-
paper writers who recently pointed out that the
seizure of Mitylene by Great Britain was
absolutely necessary under the circumstances.
In fact, the events of one week always are rough
on the “ foreign politics” leader writers of the
week previous. Russia and Hingland seem to be
eternally conspiring to make daily journalism
look hopelessly absurd. Take this maxim to heart
and carefully • consider the drivellers how they
drivel.
A deputation representing 20,000 French
workmen waited recently on the Pope, and that
holy father urged them to avoid Socialism, and
trust to “ Christianity and thrift.” Which
reminds us a good deal of that prophet with the
glass eye, 11. H. Champion, and his weary,
hollow, supercilious drawl: “You must —aw —
obey the law—ah—and pwfserve aw-dah.” In
other words, you must let things slide and pray.
But unluckily the men who let things slide and
pray are always left, and the people who trust in
the Lord and wait are left waiting.
Luya, M.L.A., who moves to abolish
payment of members in Queensland, is generally
credited with being a wealthy man. He has in
the past made whips of money at sawmilling and
land-jobbing, still owns valuable areas of
suburban lands, and has been able to build a
costly mansion for himself on a commanding hill-
site in one of the most picturesque spots on the
south side of the river. He personally doesn’t
care about the payment of members; and it is
cheap humbug for him to propose its abolition on
grounds of pretended self-abnegation.
The Australian public don’t care a rap
which side prevails in the flunkoyfied precedence-
contest between the Australian Lieutenant-
Governors and Lord Charles Scott, who claims
that they should pay him the first visit of
ceremony. The Governors and the Lieutenant-
Governors, like the Admiral, are “ the nominees ”
of a practically foreign potentate, and their
appointments are to a great extent the reward of
grovel. As the people have neither voice nor
interest in the selections, they don’t care if these
potentates never pay any visits at all.
“I am no pensioner,” observed Parkes in
the N.S.W. Assembly. But he is very likely to
become a pensioner whenever his Government
falls. The leading men on all sides have been
sounded, and there will be a big majority to vote
him a big pension—or lump sum—-whenever he
gives the wink. However, Parkes will here run a
great risk, inasmuch as it will bo competent for
any member of the House to submit reasons why
such a career as his should not be rewarded with a
pension. And what a blasting indictment could
be drawn against the man !
The recently-deceased James Russell
Lowell’s old sentiments about England ;
T. Macdonald-Paterson, a Bananaland
M.L.C. :
The compulsory registration of letters containing
valuables which exists in Great Britain is a proof that
there is good reason for it.
The existence of a State Church in England “is
a proof that there is good reason for it,” but
Macdonald-Paterson would be hooted and booted
out of Queensland were he to try and introduce
State ecclesiasticism there. Conversely, the fact
that elected English M.’sP. aren’t State paid
should be “a proof that there is no reason for”
such payment; but the Bananaland Parliament
would consume Macdonald-Paterson on a funeral
pyre of his own law-books ere it would acknow-
ledge the justice of his logic.
When the historian of the Federal move-
ment in Australia comes to estimate I uglis Clarke’s
(of Tasmania) position, he will be driven to the
sorrowful reflection that, as in Griffith’s and
Barton’s case, the bias of the special pleader and
the legal advocate who has got to justify the brief,
the whole brief, and nothing but the brief, has
overlain the original patriotic impulse. Even
bucolic Tasmania —which is at about the lowest
tide-mark of political intelligence can’t help
laughing at Clarke’s preposterous contention that
to confer upon the Senate of the proposed Federal
Legislature the power to suggest amendments, is
the same thing as giving it the power to make
amendments. Next to Griffith’s, Inglis Clarke’s
was admittedly the mt st constructive intellect of
the late Convention. May Heaven, then, help
the nation which is dependent upon the brains of
mere special pleaders for the evolution of its
charter of national freedom !
Bavousky : “ Why should marriage turn a
healthy girl into an invalid? ’
“ Oh, God, what a fearful death !” written
with charcoal on a plate, was the only sign given
by a poor devil who stowed himself away on the
steamer Wilcannia bound from London to Mel-
bourne, beneath the furnace and between the
boilers, and was roasted to death ere he could
crawl out.
I tell ye England’s law on sea and land
Hez allers been : I’ve got the heavier hand.
Of all the sarse that I can call to mind,
England does mix the most onpleasant kind ;
It’s you're the sinners allers. She’s the saint;
Wut’s good’s all English, all that’s isn’t ain’t.
She’s praised herself ontil.she fairly thinks
There ain’t no light in heaven when she winks.
She ain’t like other mortals —that’s a fact;
She never stopped the Habus Corpus Act.
She don’t put down rebellions —let’s ’em breed,
An’s allers w illin' Ireland should secede ;
She’s all that’s honest, honnable and fair ;
An’ when the Yirtoos died they made her heir.

Society
In dragging a speech out of Lady Jersey
on every possible occasion the N.S.W. public is
behaving with cruelty. The lady has obviously
not yet recovered from the effects of her severe
English illness, and when on the stump—as at
Manly the other afternoon —shows how keenly her
nerves feel the ordeal.
# # #
Lady Jersey, having seen, or heard, on
reliable authority, of certain alcoholic freaks on
the part of alleged young ladies high in Sydney
society, recently warned those concerned in unmis-
takeable terms. Notwithstanding this, a couple
of society beauties were much-malted at a big and
very fashionable hop the other night. Should
this kind of thing, coupled with midnight step-
dancing in the public streets, make much further
progress in “society,” it will really be time for
the prohibitionist women to have the franchise —
not so much for the purpose of sobering-up the
men as to stop fashionable beauties from elevating
their little fingers.
# *
“ General ” Booth says that in England he
perpetually hears the remark: “We’re going to
send Jim to Australia ; we can’t do anything with
him here.” Well, The Bulletin is on the spot
for the express purpose of preventing Jim coming
to Australia. This country doesn’t want Jim,
nor his father John either. It wants them to stay
“ at home ” and make things hot for the greedy
governing classes.
# # *
The medical men who attended a widely-
known Victorian doctor, recently deceased,
during his last illness, have “billed” the executors
to the aggregate tune of £lOOO odd. The etiquette
of the profession is that no medico charges
another; should a medico’s wife be attended, the
custom is to make a present to the officiating
brother. The executors in this case will not pay,
but leave the debt to the residuary legatees.
A harrowing catastrophe took place the
other night in the dress-circle of the Garrick
Theatre (Sydney)—the sort of catastrophe which
reminds us that all flesh is indeed grass, and all
earthly things are unreliable and transient. The
shoulder-straps of a somewhat decollete lady burst
—both of them exploded at the same moment, in
fact; and as the lady's clothes depended entirely
upon them, it followed that when the gossamer
fabric thus sent in its resignation, her attire began
to slide down rapidly. She was looking hard at
the stage and didn’t seem to notice the disaster.
Other people, however, observed the hurried
escape of her wardrobe—they saw three-fourths
of her back arise gently out of the situation,
and a good portion of her chest ; in fact,
in just about an instant there was hardly anything
of her to be seen except woman, for all the rest
went in one act. The audience grinned, barring a
professor of mathematics who was sitting just
behind the patient. He simply looked at the
undressed apparition with a smileless countenance,
and then picking up her cloak he wrapped her in
it, and she was saved. He did it as coldly as if
he had spent all his life in coming to the rescue of
women whose clothes had fallen off—he didn’t
even seem to take an interest in the job. At the
next interval a blushing lady fled hastily to the
dressing-room, holding up her attire with both
hands as she ran.
# # *
A constable, whose official duty is to guard
public buildings in Bridge-street, Sydney, and
take charge of any simpleton who offers' Parkes a
£10-note for a billot, has lost £700 by the failure
of a Sydney^ budding society. He was- teinpted
to invest in bricks and mortar by the
bait of high interest. He is the onpoliceman
who is known not to have locked up his savings
in the proverbial terrace of houses.
A titled lady, who sojourned down South
last summer, is not going there again just yet,
probably because the local folks had a big joke at
her expense. While in Hobart she had some teeth
extracted with the aid of gas, when, as conscious-
ness returned, she heaved a deep sigh and ejacu-
lated, “ Thank goodness it's over —is it a girl or a
boy ?” It was her sister who told, not the dentist.

That day happened to be visiting day with the

menced they modestly withdrew. However,
shortly after their disappearance attention was
directed to,the peculiar agitation of a window-
blind, and investigation discovered a reverend
gentleman surreptitiously enjoying the show.
Which reminds one of Mark Twain and the can-
can—“ I placed my hands before my face for very
shame. But I looked through my fingers.” A

The Eastern papers lately printed a tele-
gram concerning a horse-whipping case at Fre-
mantle, W.A. The facts appear to be these.
The local magistrate was surprised to find before
him one morning an < xalted member of society, to
wit, a stockbroker who was solid at Government
House. The charge against the broker was horse-
whipping an editor. Indignant at what he con-
sidered a reflection on his honor contained in an
obscure local print he had bought a cowhide and
given the editor “ beans.” Defendant with a smirk
admitted the assault and asked the price. The
magistrate in reply solemnly sent him up for seven
days. So far so good. But now for the sequel. The
“ jugged ” one’s friends, holding the opinion that
under responsible government the law of imprison.

next morning.
* * *
A lively’ wedding story comes fcfrom Mel-
bourne’s suburbs. The joyful event had arrived
at the refreshment stage, and all seemed bright and
fair, when the bridegroom’s father began to throw
the dejeuner about, and compass the destruction
of his son’s elect with a carving knife, on religious
grounds. Before the old man had time, however,
to fight his way through a lot of bridesmaids and
disorganised relatives of the contracting parties,
he got hemmed in by the crowd, and then the
bridegroom struck down his uplifted arm with a
boiled turkey, or something as hard, so that the
angry parent ultimately stabbed himself, and his
gore spurted forth into the tipsy-cake and things.
The sight of a deep crimson stain upon the blanc-
mange set several maiden aunts shrieking, where-
up n the bride, feeling persuaded that her
husband had been cut down like a flower in the
dawn of his honeymoon, rushed off to the kitchen
and ate a quantity of rough-on-rats, whilst the
best man and several old friends of the family
were fainting in the fender. Then the bride-
groom, finding himself on the verge of becoming
a widower, was with difficulty restrained from
falling off the roof, and the old man, who was
bleeding away in the corner, burst into remorseful
tears and asked to be supplied with a list of the
killed and wounded before he passed away.
Ultimately a change for the better set in all
round. Merciful Providence and a stomach-pump
have done a good deal for the bride, who is in a
fair way to start married life shortly. Also the
bridegroom and other guests are recovering from
the shock. Even the old man has successfully
bound up his wounds, and his bandaged frame
trembles with emotion and gratitude to Heaven
as he thinks of what might have occurred if his
murderous intentions hadn’t been intercepted by
a ham-bone, or whatever it was.
# * *
When Labertouche, of the Victorian rail-
ways, was asked whether Sara Bernhardt had a
free pass, he looked scornfully at the questioner
and said, “Certainly not.” Yet he cheerfully
admitted that Johnny Toole was franked over all
the lines in the province. Dull old Johnny, of the
baggy breeches and stale jokes, came under the
“distinguished visitor” clause, but Divine Sara,
the great actress, the real genuine celebrity, of
which Johnny is a bad Dutch-metal imitation—
O, certainly not! But the dead-head system is
not so inconsistent after all. Toole comes a good
deal nearer to the typical distinguished person
than Sara. Toole, you see, is a pal. of Albert
Edward —like the coming Admiral.
* # #
Lady Hopetoun’s brother-in-law, Acland
Hood, will stand for West Somerset at the next
llinglish elections. The young man should top
the poll in the Unionist interest. He has a neat
head, with no development where the brains ought
to be, a well-ordtred moustache, small uninterest-
ing features, low narrow brow, and a mild expres-
sionless eye. As a public speaker he chiefly shines
in “ haw !” and his politics are vaguely suspected
to be “ sound.” His coats used to wrinkle across
the shoulders a good deal when in Melbourne, but
a good London tailor will spon put that all right;
and if West Somerset doesn’t roll up with its
chuckle-headed vote for Hopetoun’s retired A.D.C.
we shall be both shocked and astonished.

The odd thing is that while “orthodox”
newspapers are so fond of asking for Booth’s
balance-sheet, and of wondering what he is doing
with his £lOO,OOO fund, they never show any
curiosity concerning the Archbishop of Canter-
bury’s £lO,OOO a-year, which is mostly expended in
loading up duchesses with early strawberries and
cream. Sauce for the General, but soup for the
“dignified ” clergy.
The influenza is often a dim, mysterious
disease whiclr comes upon public men at oppor-
tune moments. As a rule, too, it spares the piece-
worker, and concentrates its energy upon the civil
servant with a large, regular screw. It is very
little about on the first of the month, but conn s
round again a day or two before the gas-collecior
and prevents the master of the house from drawing
a cheque. At interfering with the payment of
accounts it stands unrivalled in the histc ry of
epidemics.
In Sydney, one day last week, a thin girl
with spectacles, and exceedingly hard of hearing,
was awarded £9OO for breach of promise of
marriage. There were 300 letters in the case, and
the writer of them—a scanty-looking clerk with
fair hair—seemed exceedingly surprised when
the jury—being incited thereto by the amiable
and chivalric Judge Windeyer—thudded down
their verdict. The audience also looked sur-
prised. Possibly the court was aroused to
sympathy, and its inner pathos was stirred by
the plaintiff’s spectacles; anyhow, the lady cer-
tainly got several times as much as the average
girl without spectacles can ever hope for. Juries,
however, are unreliable things at the best, and
regarded as instruments for the assessment of in-
jured feelings they don’t amount to much. The
matter, in fact, wants to be put on a business foot-
ing, and perhaps the shortest way to do this would
be by making each party put up a deposit
and sign articles for the shindy when the
engagement commences. When two pugilists
agree to fight ten rounds the money is always
deposited at the very beginning, and when two
people agree to light off and on for life, the same
course might reasonably be adopted. Then the
individual who fails to come up to the scratch for-
feits the cash, and the other one takes it, and
everything is settled. Or, if this scheme is
unworkable, each man and woman, on reaching a
marriageable age, might be inspected and valued
and duly registered by a Government official
appointed for the purpose. Then any person who
contemplates matrimony could consult the regis-
ter, on payment of the usual fee, and find out
whether he was taking risks with a £5O girl or a
£l2OO one ; and the girl who wants to throw over
some gilded youth could ascertain how much the
gymnastic feat would cost her. In either case the
money would be recoverable by summary proce-
dure. All individuals on the register could be
re-inspected and re-valued every five years, but if
either party had depreciated during the engage-
ment, the other individual should not be allowed
to take advantage of that circumstance, otherwise
there are abundance of mean individuals who
would hang on to a deteriorating girl till she was
re-inspected, and then throw her over on the
cheap. If this scheme doesn’t meet all require-
ments, we would be glad to hear of one that will.
* * *
The papers in reporting Miss Mary
MTlwraith’s marriage at Brisbane mention that
the bride carried a tasteful bouquet from the shop
of Mr. —— in Queen-street. Somehow the man
who sells the bride’s vegetable always glares forth
conspicuously in print, and everybody else is left
out. No paper ever mentions, for instance, that
the lady’s female parent was overcome by emotion
and leant up against a wall constructed in a solid
and substantial manner by John Chump, and
afterwards plastered by Patrick O’Brien; nor
does anyone state that her father afterwards fell
down a stair erected by Daniel Brown, and was
consequently confined to a bed manufactured by
the enterprising firm of Smith & Co., furniture
dealers and repairers. It would add a little
variety to the thing also, if somebody would
announce that the bride was supported on the
occasion by her youngest sister, recently spanked
in an efficient manner by that eminent instructor,
Madame Bones, whose academy is so well and
favourably known in the best circles ; but nobody
ever does announce it. The fiend that the bridal
herb was bought from looms every time, and
everybody else is nowhere.

# * #
A prominent Southern gaol-official has
just been dismissed on account of his reluctance
to part with certain private moneys and valuables
belonging to prisoners under sentence and placed
in his charge for safe keeping. He kept them so
safely that the poor wretches whose “time was
up'’ often had to leave their temporary abode
with less than they took into it. The little game
was “blown” by a man from whom a silver
bracelet had been taken. Upon demanding his
property at the expiry of his sentence he was
coolly informed by this model prison “banker”
that he had presented the bauble to his wife —
first having had her name engraved upon it.
Another case of setting a thief to catch a thief.

As “Charlie” or “Dave” Barclay goes
down Hobart streets people are accustomed to say,
“ There goes Hobart! ” The phrase will have to
be varied now to “There goes, Tasmania!”
Premier Fysh has let the cat out of the bag, and
has shown even the dullest Tasmaniac that the
Barclays and their bank, the Commercial, are
responsible for the delay in adjusting the V.D.L.
Bank trouble, and, moreover, that they will boss
Tasmania while Fysh remains at the head of the
Ministry. Fysh threatens to resign if he is com-
pelled by popular pressure to break faith with the
Commercial Bank, but as he can only keep faith
with the Commercial Bank (as to a promise which,
constitutionally, he had no right to make) by
breaking faith with Parliament and people, it is
pretty clear that he should resign anyway. And,
besides, Hobart citizens have not such short
A DREAM FACE.
[For The Bulletin.]
Growing and growing, unknown and unknowing,
In fanciful visions and feverish hours ;
By lip-touch untainted,
By painter unpainted,
Oh, face of the sainted ;
Oh, softer than sunset and fairer than flowers 1
Daunting and daunting me ever, and haunting
My nights without slumber and days without peace
In long hours of waking,
Till; daylight comes breaking
Onsvear.v eyes aching—
Oh, God, could I find her! Oh, God, could it cease !
Drifting and drifting around me and lifting
Her beckoning hands and her glorified eyes
Oh, sweet beyond seeming,
And dear beyond deeming,
Oh, face of my dreaming,
Long, long have I sought thee with yearning and sighs
Palt’ring and falling I still hear her calling
Me softly and low o’er the years unexplored.
Oh, God, but the waiting,
The search unabating.
The hunger and hating ;
Till death I must follow my own, my adored !
Brisbane.
K.
The dashing militaire
Puts pomatum on his hair,
And his game is to declare
That he wants to “ fight the Bear
But to wed a little maid,
Dash it all! the man’s afraid
So he skips to Adelaide
In a scare,
O ! the dashing militaire, &c. (dance round).

fully,

* * *
It was the death of old Jack Robertson
that killed the Sydney Reform Club. While old
Jack lived there was always hope for that whisky-
fed institution. A few years ago the Club found
itself heavily involved. It was left for Sir Jack
to suggest a remedy. “Let us drink ourselves
out of debt,” said he. And they did. Pretty
nearly every man who ventured inside the Club
was fined a case of champagne. Jack levied toll
on all-comers. Everyone who pleased him had to
shout champagne, and everyone who displeased
him had also to shout champagne. And from the
ukases which proceeded from his throne in the
eastern corner of the general room there was no
appeal.

that “Thunderbolt’s boy” Thompson, now doing

bird,” which follows him about within the
Stockade.

A N.S.W. civil servant with £6OO a year
was recently sued for 30s. Execution having been
issued, the warrant was returned nulla bona.
Exasperated, the creditor waited upon the head
of the department who simply endorsed the
warrant “ pay or resign.” The C.S. paid.

Advertisement from the Toowoomba (Q.)
Chronicle
PUBLIC NOTICE.
•VTY Wife, Annie Eliza, having returned to her home,
M we are now living happily again.
HENRY ,
, August 24, 1891.
In other words, Henry can now come home with-
out having the bootjack dashed at his head, and
instead of looking scornfully at the dinner and
then saying “ Damn !” and knocking the meat on
the floor, he consumes the victuals harmoniously ;
also, Annie Eliza has ceased to get hot tea in her
eye unexpectedly. In consequence of which the
cat’s tail, which was much broken and trodden on
in many domestic forays, is healing up again, and
the animal has got kittens; and the small, scared,
red offspring, which used to bolt for the back door
whenever the row started, is calmer now, and
snuffles peacefully, with a large slice of bread and
jam, in the corner. And, taking it all round, an
aspect of calm, domestic bliss pervades the scene,
to which Henry has been so long a stranger that
he feels he must advertise about it.

The W.A. goldfields are being boomed
with a vengeance. A S.M. Herald telegram
announces that on the Murchison “ two men
named Peterkin and M‘Pherson have quite
400 cwt. of solid gold.”
If the letter of an “ influential person ” to
Sydney Morning Herald, placing a value of
£5OO upon some note-books of Oswald Brierly, the
marine painter, who visited the Australias in
H.M.S. Rattlesnake a generation agone, induces
the Government to purchase those same pencil-
hnors, a monstrous job will be effected. They can
possess no antiquarian or historical interest, and
their literary and artistic value is brought to a
very depressed plane indeed, by the circumstance
that if they had been worth publication, they
would have been published. A key to the pro-
posed transaction may be found in the fact that
Brierly was the Queen’s painter, or something.
When the alleged |“ leading journal” of
the mother-colony of the Austi’alias dishes up old-
time incidents, the least that can be expected is
an approximation to accuracy of date, and evi-
dence of a faculty of research of a degree that
would be expected from a school boy competing
for a Carruthers’ prize-essay. The Bulletin
hereby informs the S.M. Herald that the first
dramatic performance in Sydney was not that of
“ The Revenge ” in 1790, but was on the occasion
of the presentment of the comedy of “The Re-
cruiting Officer,” and the tragedy of “The Fair
Penitent,” on 4th June, 1789.
* # #
About four more unchartered banks have
burst in Sydney within the last week. Most of
them went very quietly ; they have been so near
the ground for months past that they had only a
short distance to fall. They all went very much
the same way. Somebody whose fixed deposit was
due wanted to get his money back, and despite
the impassioned oration of the curled and oiled
“ teller ” he refused to re-fix the cash for another
three years. Then the teller went and told the
manager, and as there was nothing in the cash-
box but 3s. and a used stamp, the manager also
delivered an eloquent speech to the customer, and
adjured him by the memory of his aged mother
to leave that deposit in. When he wouldn’t do
it, the “board” went out and tried to raise the
money from another bank,.and couldn’t get it.
Then it burst. It would have borrowed the char-
woman’s savings to go on with if it could have
got them, but the charwoman, in many cases, had
been paid in scrip, and hadn’t any money in con-
sequence ; and even the office-boy’s wages were on
fixed deposit; and the butcher had got debentures
for the office cat’s-meat, and there was absolutely
nothing left to borrow anywhere.
* * *
History repeats itself. An item from
New York tells of a goldsmith who regularly
played the trick of wearing a well-greased thatch,
fingering gold-dust, and wiping his fingers on his
hair, thus carrying away nightly a good deal of
gold, and who is now in gaol. Old residents will
remember the hirsute bank-manager of a
N.S.W. up-country town, who, when a digger
came to sell gold, used to finger it carelessly, f
and then, with some simple remark, such as,
“It seems very light gold,” would carelessly
place his hands on his head and adopt ]
a thoughtful Napoleonic attitude. Then

LADY TEACHES (to class); “Now, who can form a sentence containing the word

B RIGHT BOY: “I will ‘trump it’ with a spade.”
he would weigh the digger’s dust, pay for it,
and bow him out. One day, a digger suddenly
returned to ask some question. Not seeing the
manager behind the counter, and the sitting-room
door being open, the digger walked right on and
in. He found Mi'. Manager dusting his head with
his hands, the gold falling in a fine shower upon a
spread-out paper disposed for its reception. That
acquisitive dodger was duly “ sent up.”

Gorgeous male millinery—or, should it be
called “third-sex” millinery?—was one of the
cardinal features of the Anglican General Synod
last week. Primate Saumarez-Smith was dazzling
in pink and scarlet robes cut bias, with puffed
paniers of rissoles and jambon a la Mayonnaise
rampant, with a salad of clerical fixings and
oecumenical feuilles setting forth his ample
physique dla bonbon de fatted-calf. Bishop Goe,
of Melbourne, appeared in cassock, stole and
hood, lightened up with wrinkles couchant and
upper-lip peduncular. The aesthetic Bishop Ken-
nion, of Adelaide, dittoed the dress items, but
contented himself with a smile supine with
aureolar variations. Bishop Montgomery, of
Tasmania, the Antinous of the. Synod, vied
in Cambridge tints with the Primate, with
frillings of shot salad and shell saladin.
The Bishops of Goulburn and of Perth donned
black silk- D.D.’s gowns, with scarlet Hoods,
knock-kneed en train, morticed with interdigita-
tions of pickled swansdown a la agapemone. A
lot of Oxford men wore black and scarlet, variously
decorated with petits pains au caviare, puffed out
fully with whiff - whaffs of cowslip pleats
reticulated with horse-collars jaccnt. No bustles
were detected. Every Bishop had his own
dressing-case cn evidence and plagiedral, with anti-
periodical supports astraddle. The Bulletin’s
man-milliner has offered to swear an affidavit to
the above allegations, which, howevei’, present
such a palpable impress of truth as to render the
taking of an oath unnecessary.

One Bevil Granville Carteret Thynn , au
ex-officer of the militia, an eldest son of a landed-
proprietor and a relative of the Marquis of Bath
and the Dowager Duchess of Buecleuch, 24 years
of age, recently deposed in a London Bankruptcy
Court that in 12 mouths “a lady with whom he
was living ” had cost him £7169. His father had
refused to keep him unless he went out to one of
the colonies. Blessed kawlinies !

# # *
Here is an original essayette on Mar-
riage. We do not give the writer’s name, lest
harm befall him. Australia can ill spare so
sprightly a young man :
Those who rail against marriage have either been
jilted or accepted.
Marrying for love is a white elephant. It is sacred, but
it eats its head off.
Marrying for money is an equal blending of avarice and
gold. It is the basest alloy known. Even the supreme
sovereign is only one-twelfth copper,
Love and silver form the combination that emits the
sweetest tone.
Love is to marriage what illness is to death. Those of
a melancholy temperament sicken and succumb most
readily.
Some get married because they think it is the best way
to live. This is like the fasting man—starving himself to
earn his daily bread.
Some get married for a helpmeet. They are like
hbi’ses -they go best when well driven.
To many love is a state of slavery with jealousy as
overseer.
Although many say marriage is a failure, none say
love is.
There is only one thing better or worse than marriage.
That is Death. Yet those on the brink of either cherish
the same hope—the hope of a better future.
A bride is wonderfully like a corpse. She is clothed in
white and “looks happier than she ever did before.”
A scholar-wife is often like a china bull-dog. Only good
for frightening friends.
Don’t marry a political gorgon. She holds on her
course like a tram-car. When you attract most notice,
then you are most like a dog harking in front of it.
The Church view of matrimony is like the keepers’ view
of the bear that was clawing the poor Frankfort woman
to death. Too precious to be interfered with.
Asking the reason why your wife is angry is examining
a blasting charge. It goes off. There is always safety iti
distance.
Those who agree upon everything before marriage
quarrel about nothing after marriage. This is because
love is everything before marriage—nothing after.
Some marry early, some late in life, others not at all.
Whichever you do, either of the remaining two will seem
preferable.
To marry a woman because she is clever is to pal with
the devil.
Marrying in haste is to be a fish. You snap the bait,
and if you get divorce the hook stops in your jaw. Marry-
ing deliberately is much the same thing.
All the difference between marriage and death is about
two per cent. Ninety-eight per cent, marry, one hundred
per cent. die.

We see through the game of the Women’s
Christian Temperance Union, which wants to
deprive man of his grog, barmaids, racing, late
hours, tobacco, slogging, and Sunday (and other{
amusements. Man’s money is in future to be
saved up for the purchase of millinery and
bassinettes ; his out-of-office-hours energy is to be
devoted solely to the— er—expansion of humanity.
# * *
All South Australians remember wealthy,
wicked, dirty, ignorant, old Crowe, whose estate
has been feeding the lawyers for years past. They
may also remember that his daughter—the darling
of ins miserable old heart—was killed by a fall
from her horse, and that when her grieving parent
arrived at the scene of the catastrophe he shook
the corpse once or twice before saying, “ Yes,
she’s as dead as a maggot.” A magnificent
monument, topped by an angel in white marble,
was erected over Miss Crowe in the Robe church-
yard. Dear father “ stood ” £BOO for this work of
art, and he went down one morning with the
monumental mason who had taken the order, to
see what sort of value he was getting for his
money. The one poetical incident in the whole
history of the family comes in right here. The
dead girl had been the only thing at all resembling
an angel that ever smiled upon the old man. And
as he came near to the tall, white-winged figure
over his daughter’s grave, a darned black crow
flew down from the clouds and perched itself upon
the angel’s head—an emblem of the name that the
original had often blushed for. The human Crowe
blew his nose uneasily, and admitted that “It
looked as though the bird had done it for the
purpose.”

An old woman named Howe came down
from Woodend (Vie.) to Melbourne to join in
welcoming General Booth, and died at the meet-
ing. Howled herself to death, we presume!
Shrieked and jumped herself into eternity !
Hallelujah !
# * *
Wherever, in the vicinity of Sydney, there
is a particularly dangerous and deadly railway-
crossing, it is an absolutely certain thing that
there is an undertaker’s shop right opposite. The
man of hearses loves to haunt such places, and
when he has erected his death’s-head sign, and put
a baby-coffin in the window, he stands about on
the footpath, ready at any hour to rush out
when somebody is squashed by a ponderous
engine, and measure the remains for their
wooden hide, and take an order for the last
resting place. If the deceased is so utterly
shattered that he can’t be moved except in a
jug then the nearest undertaker is bound to get
the job. And round the corner from that dealer
in deaders and skulls there is generally to be
found a tombstone merchant, who is also waiting
to bolt across and secure the order for the broken
pillar and the partially naked figure to go above
the squashed one’s dust. The presence of these
two persons is a great comfort to the nervous
passenger; when he sees a Baldwin engine bearing
down on him he knows that kind, loving hands
will gather up the wreckage, and measure them
on the spot, and that all will be for the best.

The other day a N.S. Wales Government
clerk had to write officially to a Legislative Coun-
cillor named (say) Brown, whom he addressed as
“The Honorable Richard Burlington-Brown,
Esq., C.M.G., M.L.C.” “I presume,” said the
clerk to his superior officer, “ that this address
will be all right ?” “Yes, give him all his titles—
he is touchy about them,” replied the boss. Next
day the letter was returned by the Hon. R. 8.8.,
&c., with a memo, on the turned-down corner :
“Is not the title ‘Esquire’ a surplusage in this
connection ?” As if any title, however common-
place, could have been surplusage in such a “ con-
nection !”

THE “SALVATION” DRUMMER’S HYMN.
I’m a ’allelujah shouter,
Which I castergates the drum,
And I thump the h’erring doubter
Into blessed Kingdom Come ;
They say our grammer’s ’azy,
And our music’s rather rough,
But I’ll send the devil crazy
If I whack away enough.
O, the General’s a-telling
Of his mishun here below,
While the multitoodes are yelling
And a-swaying to an’ fro :
We are bringing life eternal
To the loafer in the slum,
And that Satan chap infernal
Has to flee before the drum.
Our system of behaving
Seems a bit like “ makin’ game,”
But the souls we keep on saving
Always gets there just the same,
Our “glib and h’uncshus story ”
May be blasfemy to some,
Yet there’s dozens drove to Glory
By the ’allelujah drum.
You seldom find them clerics,
Who are quite the real cheese,
Get the women in hysterics
And the men upon their knees;
Our good effects are fleeting
Very orfen, we allow,
Still the drum is always beating,
And there’s virtue in the row.
Bang, bang ! for our Commander,
And another bang for luck ;
They can chaff, condemn, or slander—
But they can’t deny his pluck ;
He treats the ’ardened sinner
Like a brother and a chum,
Ay, and calls the prigs to dinner
With a thump upon the drum.
There’s a mob of parsons preaching
Holy ’ash that’s awful dry,
All the hearts that they are reaching
You could shove ’em in your eye;
With religion they are messing
And might just as well be dumb,
But we’ve got the Father’s blessing
On the ’allelujah drum.
. See, what a dreadful story’s told,
As bad a tale as one could tell —
That, miners in the search for gold,
Will venture to the Gate of Hell.
Lor, if they find that rush a snide,
We’ll bet a thou., and plank the same,
They’ll bust the Gate and swarm inside.
And jump Old Nick’s prospecting claim.
There’s gold in Heaven, for ain’t we told,
In language which should hearts compel,
That all its streets are paved with gold ?
And now we’re told there’s gold in Hell.
In Earth, Heav’n, Hell, it seems they use it,
This bait for passions homicidal ;
Well—so—if God, man, devils, choose it,
Then gold should be a worthy idol.

T IS INCONVENIENT,
UNPROFITABLE,
AND PARTICULARLY FOOLISH
To buy rubbish of any sort. If the best article of
its class cannot be obtained in one place it can in
another, and it is always bad policy to be bluffed
into buying the second best of anything, just for
the sake of effecting a temporary saving of either
tune or money. For the saving
and regrets, like curses, come home to roost.
This advice has a good, all round, general ap-
plication, but it is rarely heeded because it is so
cheap and common. The foolish housewife
listens to it and smiles, but vaunteth to herself
meanwhile her superior wisdom, -while the wise one
closes one eye in silent meditation, and scores a
notch upon the handle of the broom or the head
of her nearest offspring, whereby she may after-
wards refresh her memory
In no sense is this well-seasoned hint of so much
value as in its application to the purchase of oil
for domestic use, and in the best interests of every
consumer this should be cut out and pasted in the
hat of the head of every household. Never buy
inferior oil until you have discovered an insurance
company that will
And even then it would be an unwise thing to do,
because as the time, place, and probabilities of
the risk are uncertain, the premium must be
abnormally high. Higher even than the wreckage
might be expected to go, and the contemplation
of that alone should be sufficient to impress the
merits of Luxene, the new illuminant, on the
minds of all Christian peoples.
LUXENE HAS NO SMELL,
GIVES A BEAUTIFUL LIGHT,
IS VERY ECONOMICAL,
AND UNQUESTIONABLY SAFE.
Try it, and be for ever grateful for the advice,
which is as disinterested as it is sound.
IS ONLY TEMPORARY,
AND PROFIT BY IT.

LOST TREASURE.
Mr. Win. Morgan, Chairman of the Pukekohe
(N.Z.)East Highway Board, writes as follows:
“ During harvesting operations in January last
one of my boys had the misfortune to lose his
Waterbary, but where he had no idea. He
searched the paddock, but could not discover the
lost treasure. A few days ago, however, one of
our neighbours picked it up, wound it, and it
started to go at once, although it had been lying
on the ground exposed to the weather for two
months. Surely such a fact is worthy of record
for the popular Waterbury.”

KEEP IT FOR REFERENCE
The following suggestions are not designed to
induce the public to attempt the duties of the
regular surgeon, but merely to place the reader in
possession of a means of treatment of the minor
accidents occurring daily in the household. No
family can be assured against accidents; the
broken bone, tiie dislocated joint, the severed
artery, these are misfortunes that may possibly
happen to anyone; and we scarcely need to add
that they demand the instant attention of the
most skilful surgeon within reach. But by house-
hold accidents we mean those which are constantly
occurring every day, in nearly every family, and
which, while not always dangerous in themselves,
are exceedingly annoying. Burns, bruises, cuts,
scalds, sprains, &c., are principal among these
troublesome and annoying occurrences, and
demand immediate treatment with the best means
at hand. In the kitchen, the dining hall, the
nursery, and the sitting room they are liable to
happen, and instead of fear and alarm at the
sight of the cut or smashed finger, or burned or
bruised arm, or scalded surface, a cool and quiet
manner should be assumed, and after washing
away the blood (if required), the injured parts
should be dressed with that most valuable remedy
~St. Jacobs Oil. Its especial worth for the
injuries named, and others of a similar nature, is
not based upon mere claim and assertion ; but its
constant use for a long term of years has given it a
reputation which is alwa3's increasing. Its sur-
prisingly quick relief, its cleansing properties, its
tendency to quickly remove all inflammation, and
its wonderful efficacy in the above, as well as in
all muscular and other pains, such as rheumatism,
neuralgia, toothache, headache, stiffness of the
limbs, joints, &c., these qualities render St. Jacobs
Od pre-eminently the best external remedy before
the people, which claim is fully substantiated by
the class of strong testimony voluntarily accorded
it by representatives of every class and station in
life.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Seven Melbourne bookmakers have beensum-
moned for allow ing llieir tobacconist shops to be used for
betting purposes. Most people thought they were allow-
ing their betting shops lo be used for the purchase of
tobacco.
• # •
The most recently prosecuted Sydney totalisator-
nmn has been fined ±'loo, including court costs. The
magistrate hesitated long ere deciding hot to send him to
gaol.
* # «
Dick Barker, 7ft., and Joe Delore, Gft. Gin.,
were the two infants who faced each other at Sydney
Olympic Club on Saturday night “to a finish.” It ap-
peared any odds on Delore at the start, hut Barker stayed
the longest, and knocked his friend out in the third
round. Ben Gaunt was the longest Englishman who ever
got into the ring, and when lie fought Bendigo the latter
was too smart for the long ’un, and would land a stinger
and drop immediately on his knees. This so infuriated
the giant that, upon Bendigo landing an extra hard one
and dropping tauntingly, Gaunt could not contain himself,
hut boxed Bendigo's ears, thus losing on a foul.
# # *
There are some men in the N.S.W. Parliament
now who were “no mugs with their hands” in bygone
days. “Jim” Garvan was no pick-up with the gloves,
and few men on the Castlereagh or the Barwon could take
a fall out of George Cass. Gedrge always carried a bit too
much beef, and qsed to go in and try to smash his man up
while his wind lasted, his whirlwind act usually coming
to a successful conclusion. One of George’s hardest mills
was at a town on the Namoi when he knocked out a noted
bully in short order by the light of three tallow candles,
held respectively by the police-magistrate and two Jus-
tices of the Peace.

Goldsborough and Marvellous head the list of
winning sires for the season, from August Ist, 1890, to
31st July, 1891, with a total of 28 each, Grandmaster
following closely with 26. In re value of stakes, Musket
comes out ahead by the help of ids wounderful son
Carbine, his stock having won in stakes over £17,500.
* * *
Although Dr. Carver was unsuccessful in the
recent match with Mr. L. Clarke, it was not bad shooting
on his part that lost him the match, as, out of his hun-
dred birds, he only actually missed one, several badly-hit
birds just managing to get out of bounds. The Doctor
was in great form at Lady Robinson’s Beach the other
day, when he accounted for 51 birds (regular hummers)
out of 52.
* * *
As The Bulletin has often pointed out of late
years, the only chance for the Hawkeshury Race Club is
their obtaining the privilege of holding their future
meetings prior to the Randwick gathering, as under the
present state of things the old institution is in a rapid
decline. In former days there used to be heavy betting
on the events weeks before, but now scarcely a wager is
hooked prior to the day of the race. This year the pre-
sence of H. Oxenham’s gelding Hero in the County Purse
a* the handy weight of 7st. 91b., including penalty,
reduced the field to eight starters, and there can be no
gainsaying the fact that a worse field never faced the flag
in the event. Hero certainly did not run up to his recent
Randwick form, but the course was exceptionally heavy
and not suited to h's long, striding style of going.

Long Jim Hall, the Australian middleweight, is
probably the most scientific man—bar Peter Jackson-
Australia has yet turned out. In Chicago, before some
300 people, Hall and Corbett boxed four rounds, when
Hall showed vast superiority over his opponent. Mr.
Steve O’Donnell, the Sydney atnateur, a pupil of Peter
Jackson, is a boxing wonder, and the writer doubts if
anyone living could give him a start for four scientific
rounds

• • •
Mick Ives’ mill with Jim Barron, at Sydney
Gymnastic Club, was most disappointing. Ives appa-
rently showed the white feather and went out in the
eighth round. And yet he had previously been seen to
advantage with the gloves, and in several turns-up with
the knuckles had proved himself of good material. How-
ever, he did not seem in good condition on the occasion.
Whatever the cause, the splendidly-built little fellow will
find it easier to gain the reputation of a “ cocktail ” than
to lose it.
• • #
There is a story told of two horses trained at
Newmarket, of such equal merit that when they were
ridden by two lads of the same weight, they invariably
ran a dead heat, but if one of the boys carried the key of
the stable in his pocket, his opponent’s horse won by a
short head. Next !
* * *
There nearly eventuated a riot at one of the
Sydney pedestrian grounds, a few nights ago, in connec-
tion with the running of a professional handicap. In the
final heat the favourite, who went out at two to one on,
appeared to win comfortably by half-a-yard, but to the
dismay of his backers the judge decided against him.
There was some talk afterwards of matching the pair to
run for £5O from the same marks, but when it came to
staking the money the backers of the man who had
received the stakes declined to “ plank.”
# # *
Poor H. H. Simpson, the fine young American
who came to Sydney with Spalding’s baseball team, is
dead. The cause of trouble was the relapse of an old
rupture, received in playing baseball. “*
# # #
There was an excited “Nap” party the other
evening in the Adelaide express. They numbered eight
altogether; they were playing a shilling game, and at
last there was a treble-header on and a big pool. Just
then the train stopped, and one of the players—a stolid
old man with a cold, hard eye-went out to get a hurried
drink. While he was away the others put up a base
deception upon him with a view to taking him in. They
dealt him a hand consisting of ace, king, queen, knave,
and nine, and gave another player a hand consisting of
the ten, six, four, three, and deuce. Then they smiled
softly as they thought how the old veteran with the cold,
hard eye would be inflamed at the sight of his hand, and
how he would go “nap” wildly and tumultuously, being
goaded on by the thought of the big pool, and then how
he would fall with a crash and a curse on the last card and
sit swearing among the debris of his shattered fortunes,
while they collected from him 15s. each. They were still
thinking of these things and getting cramps through
smothered laughter when the ancient one came back and
picked up his cards. Then with a dull and fish-like ex-
pression of utter unconcern he went four, and took from
them 12s. each, and the faces of the company fell so
violently that they nearly fell off altogether, and the
event cast a gloom over the entire journey,

the price of the Orchestral Organette
(our latest production) over other
instruments in the market, we have
succeeded in producing the very ACME
OF MUSICAL INVENTION, an instru-
ment with as much variety of tone as an
organ costing £25. The illustration gives
you but a faint idea of general make and
finish, but every Orchestral Organette is
supplied with 28 FULL SIZED
AMERICAN ORGAN REEDS, the same
size and quality as those used in a cabinet
organ. The reeds are placed in a novel
manner over a double suction bellows,
and are controlled by THREE STOPS,
as follows, viz., Flute, Expression and
Vox Humana. The music is produced by
perforated sheets, which pass around the
Organette in endless bands, enabling
a tune to be played over and over
again without stopping, furnishing
the GRANDEST ORCHESTRAL
EFFECTS, either in sacred, secular,
dance, or vocal music, affording a rich,
sonorous, and powerful accompaniment
to the voice, requiring absolutely no skill
in the performer, and THE RANGE OF
MUSIC AND TONE IS PRACTICALLY
UNLIMITED. By the manipulation of
the stops, a tone as soft and sweet as a
zephyr, or a loud, long and swelling
melody may be produced; trills and high
falsetto, as well as reverberating bass,
and all manner of pleasing combinations
at the will of the performer.

Orchestral Organettes into every town

impossibility, and the most difficult operatic air is played with as much ease as the most simple hymn. In many homes will be

Some years ago, Phil. Garland, a well-
known North Queenslander, had the luck to find
an aboriginal mummy forked in a tree after the
autocthonal fashion. He annexed it, of course,
and was conveying it into Cairns. He camped
one night at Alley’s, which was then a tent only.
At this very time Sam Griffith and the late Billy
Miles were making from Herberton to Cairns, and
they also got belated at Alley’s. Alley roused out
Phil., who was a Griffithite at that period, and
readily turned out to oblige the Liberal leaders,
but thoughtlessly left his precious mummy on one
of the stretchers. At sparrow-chirp next morning
Phil, suddenly thought of his deceased. Entering
the tent without ceremony, he asked Sam if he
had noticed a corpse kicking around. Billy Miles
rushed out of the tent at once ; Griffith only sat
up and asked, as if in cross-examination—“What
corpse ?” “My corpse,” quoth Phih —“ a corpse
of high degree. . . Ah ! here it is, I see you’ve
been using it as a pillow,” “But what is it?”
“ A blackfellow’s mummy I found, and I mean to
sell it to some Museum or other.” “ I thought I
smelt something of very high degree,” murmured
Samuel, “but put it down to malarial emanations
from the soil,”

The Argus “ social gossip ”-er sends a
cock-and-bull yarn concerning blackmail levied
upon London managers by critics, and “leaders
of the ‘ educated pit,’” who plant original trage-
dies and comedies upon the boss and expect him
to buy them right out. A stupider lie never got
into a Tory publication. Influential critics may
occasionally rush a manager into paying for a play
he doesn’t require, or dream of producing, but the
suggestion that “almost every critic” sends in
these dummy plays “with the regularity of the
rate papers,” is hare-brained bosh. Yet it has
more colour of possibility than the story that
certain imaginary educated pittites “have it in
their power by organising a sort of first-night riot
to paralyse the powers of the players, and thus
bring about a fiasco.” Eirst-night riot—first-night
rot! The man writes as though he doesn’t know
that Australia is about one-third full of ex-London
theatre-goers, many of them quite modern. These
latter are perfectly well aware of what would
happen to the “leaders ”—ye gods !—who started
a sort of riot in the pit (of all places) in order to
spoil an interesting show. Why, the others would
knock the leaders silly and then sit on them so as
to get a better view. There is but one rational
explanation of the Argus man’s yarn. No doubt
he once had a ghastly play of his own produced
for a single consecutive matinee, and the pit gave
it slops. That’s what’s the matter with Jacob.

Miss Eva Booth, daughter of the General*
after vainly expostulating with the Mayor of
Eastbourne (Eng.) against the local persecution
of the Salvation Army, went down on her knees
and prayed that his heart might be softened.
Whereupon, the mayor, a God-fearing Scotchman,
fell upon his marrow-bones also, and prayed that
anew heart might be vouchsafed to the “law-
breakers.” The knee-drill thus ended in a drawn
battle.
Dear Bulletin, —That you, above all,
should lend your breath to puff that bladder,
A. Patchett Martin, a degree or two bigger !
Why don’t you prick it once and for all? That
Murray’s Magazine article on Gordon was a
mere rechauffe, of poor Tenison Woods’ Victorian
Review “ Notes,” Sutherland’s contributions to
Argus, Australasian and Melbourne Review,
and Hewlett Ross’ pretentious and inaccurate
bit of book-making, “ The Poet of the Centaurs”
—how did this fact escape you? In your par.
you attach to Martin the merit —poor, perhaps,
but still a merit—of unearthing original communi-
cations as to Gordon’s Australian career. Nothing
of the sort—nothing can be more remote, ap-
parently, from Martin’s method of work than
personal research or enquiry. His paper is
diluted Woods, tinctured by Sutherland and Ross
—the work of which last was wiped out of the
category of trustworthy biographical data by half-
a-dozen lines from Gordon’s widow—now Mrs.
Peter Low. There is absolutely nothing original
in it except its blunders—-a characteristic that
prpves its kinship to the Temple Bar article of
seven years ago which was the vehicle chosen by
the author of “Fernshaw,” to introduce himself
and Gordon—don’t alter the order of the words—
to English readers. The Temple Bar screed was
illumined by the wonderful discovery by Martin
of tiie “hitherto unpublished” MS. of “The
Exile’s Farewell,” a peerless triumph of research
and industry which was only slightly discounted
by the fact that these particular verses are to be
found in the small and rare volume of Gordon’s
Poems, published at Mount Gam bier, S.A.,
twenty-odd years before! Of course, Martin
never heard of the Mount Gambier issue, and in
this respect he is not singular among the poet’s
critics and biographers. Not one in fifty of ’em
has heard of it. But —in presence of this simple
fact —what a farce it is that these parasites of our
growing Australian literatui'e should be permitted
to float in the irridescenoe diffused by a strong
writer’s fame ! And, O Heavens ! to think that it
is A. Patchett Martin who guides Literary
Opinion ! The noble function performed erst-
while by a Dilke and a .Terdan to fall into the
hands of the author of “ Fernshaw ! A Hire-
ling Scribe.
