“ Bhei ney ”is defined in a new American
dictionary (run by an Irishman) as a “shrewd
fellow—hence a Jew.” The editor of the
American Hebrew asks why “Mick” was left
out. “Mick,” he suggests, “a low fellow—hence
an Irishman.”

time of the great collision is said to have been an

approaching smash would furnish some good
“copy.” Who knows? A man’s professional
instincts are often too strong for him.
A certain wealthy Gwydir district, N.S. W.,
squatter has his sheep mustered by blacks, and
shorn by Chinese. There are 40 stands in the
shed, but the squatter keeps about 70 Chinkies on
hand, allowing a 75 per cent, surplus for deaths,
desertions, opium - trances and lepers. The
Pastoralists’ Association, in the first flush of the
new Conference compromise with the Shearers’
Union, has repudiated the squatter and expelled
him from the shade of its umbrella. This may be
fairly classed as a fresh shearers’ triumph.
Dear Bulletin, —Your paragraph upon
the humours of the Australian sub-contract might
lead people to believe that wages-men slipped up
by a levanting sub-contractor are without a
remedy in all the provinces. It may be useful to
point out that, by the N.S.W. Contractors’ Debts
Act, 1879, a contractor sub-letting any part of his
work is responsible to the extent of two months’
pay for the wages of the workman employed by
the sub-contractor, and a workman employed by
a sub contractor may proceed under the Act
against the contractor as if he had been directly
employed by him. The amending Act of 1888
extends this remedy to the case of persons wl o
have supplied material to the sub-contractor.—
Yours, Unionist.

Let it be distinctly understood that this is no
scheme of quackery to draw money out of the
afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent medicine
humbug, since there is not a grain of medicine of any
kind required in the treatment. Notwithstanding
this fact, it takes directly hold of the worst cases
of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-
aches, heart disease, incipient consumption,
diabetes, and Bright’s disease of the kidneys, in-
cluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus alloiving
nature herself to do her work unimveded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation without the least danger to the most
delicate constitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to, which will not be
affected favorably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.

may rightfully be compared to the ‘bloom of youth,*
though he is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in my prac-
tice in the twenty years past, have convinced me that
great practical benefit may be derived by members of the
medical profession and others from a careful study of the
rationale of the treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the
details of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely be due to him for
whatever scientific advances he may be able to unfold in
the line of assisting nature to ward off disease, and in the
restoration of health.-R. F. Stevens, M.D.”

The distinguished pulpit-orator and evangelist,

October 7 :
“ ***Touching your wonderful discovery, I take much

Tales of Old Sydney.
No. XII.—“ The Hanging of the Eight.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A July sun parted feebly and sullenly the
sombre clouds which dropped a drizzle upon the
town; and the Reverend Mr. Cowper and the
Reverend Father Therry, meeting at the lumber-
yard gate, shook hands in a manner that showed
their susceptibility to the depressing influences of
the atmospheric gloom.
“A miserable day, Father ! ” was the Church of
England Chaplain’s salutation. “But not in-
appropriate to the, ah—the event.”
“Well, Mr. Cowper, I must say that for the poor
ifellows inside I would have preferred a brighter
■sunshine. If men like ourselves yield readily in
temper and mood to the character of the day,
what wonder is it that those poor half-brutes
■■should be still more impressed by damp and
igloom, or exhilarated by warmth and sunshine.”
“None, indeed ! ” sighed Mr. Cowper. “ They
are, indeed, little more than animals, and as such
love to bask in the sunshine and shrink from wet
and darkness. How are yours ? ”
“Ah, poor boys, they were in a bad way when
I left ’em yester eve. And that Moore callant —
any heart bleeds for him—l believe he is inno-
>cent ! ”
“ You really think so, Father—how awful if it
should so prove later. You’ve seen the Governor,
■of course ? ”
“Yes, but it’s the old story, he said. There
ns never a sessions which leaves a string of con-
■demned but what I have the same tale to tell, and
Teally he would beg leave to be excused this time,
‘having already reprieved fourteen, from extending
'the prerogative to another soul of this batch.”
“ But why should he not have reprieved Moore,
if there are doubts ?”
“In the first place, the doubts have no legal
value. Doubts are not proofs, and, in the second
place ” The priest lowered his head to
whisper into the other ecclesiastic’s ear.
The Reverend Mr. Cowper started back.
“ Lot!” he ejaculated, the sharp note expressive
of horror giving the key to his intonation.
“Lot! I thought all such practices had been
(abandoned!”
Father Therry had lifted his cane to beat
the wicket. Now, he re-lifted it. The
action was simple enough, but there was some-
thing in the gesture suggestive of accusation and
-denunciation. It was as though he was hurling
an anathema against the Evil Powers. Perhaps
die was.
“ Abandoned ! So did I! But I know this for
iafaet. I was waiting to see the secretary in the
ante-room, which is the next room to the clerks’.
They did not know I was there —and they went
»on in their devilish game !”
“ Father !”
“ On my oath, as a priest, Cowper, it was so!
fßut, perhaps, it’s the word ‘devilish ’ you object
cto? Isn’t the tiring devilish, and shouldn’t the
,proper word be used for the thing ?”
“ The thing is—devilish !” assented the scarcely
iless-heroic but much less fiery Protestant Chap-
Hain. “ But I could not conceive that such things
were still done ! In the olden days I knew they
were, but now !”
They’ll always be done, Cowper. I
Tdlßyou, while the men at home deal with men’s
lives with Jess thought .than they give to their
dogs and horses—while they think a head of game
is worth more than ah immortal soul* and a human
life of less value than a few pence worth of pro-
perty. Done ! It was done on Friday ! It will
be done for many Fridays yet!”
The wicket in the huge gates now swung open,
and the two clergymen, divided by unbridgeable
differences of faith, but alike in largeness of heart
and purity of conduct, went in to administer the
last consolations of their respective creeds to the
Condemned.
For this morning was that of an Execution
Monday, and Eight had been left, and fourteen
had been withdrawn by the vice-regal mercy in
conjunction with the turn of the dice-box.
# # *
Twenty-two criminals, without, as it hap-
pened, including a solitary murderer in their
number, had been cast for death at the last
sessions of the Supreme Court. And fourteen
had been reprieved.
On what principle the reprieve had been granted
no one, unless it was the Governor himself and
the Colonial Secretary, and the Judge who had
passed the sentences, knew. At least, that was
the popular, the general, impression. As a matter
of unambiguous fact, it is exceedingly improbable,
however, that even the three great men just
referred to were any more precisely informed than
the unpotential mob outside official circles. If it
had been possible to put such august personages
into the witness-box, the only answer that could
have been obtained would, to a certainty, have
been that no principle at all had guided the vice-
regal hand in “pricking the remissions.” Pure
chance ruled the game, and, as men’s lives were
the stakes, perhaps Father Therry’s epithet of
devilish was not altogether misapplied.
Fourteen were taken and the Eight were left;
and this is the manner of the taking.
* * *
Judge’s report, sir !” greeted the Colonial
Secretary, as he called in his Under-Secretary.
“Sessions closed, then? How many capital?”
questioned the great man.
“Twenty-two, sir, and five death-recorded.”
“ That all ? Then we’ll not require an extraor-
dinary Council. The regular meeting will do.
Make usual enquiries as to special circumstances
attaching to any of the Condemned which would
warrant his Excellency exercising his preroga-
tive.”
“Very well, sir. Any thing else ?” The Under-
secretary was minuting the instructions.
How many did you say ? Twenty-two ?”
“Yes, sir.”
“ Then instruct the Sheriff to prepare for, say,
eight or nine. ” ,
“ Shall I get warrants ready in blank for that
number, then ? ”
The Colonial Secretary and Registrar of
Records assented—and passed on to consider some
tenders for the supply of handcuffs. There was
an hour’s, work in the tenders. But five minutes
then and twenty later on would polish off twenty-
two condemned.
* , • •
When Mr. Under-Secretary Harrington
retired to his own room, he called in his immediate
subordinate, and passed on the instructions.
“As to the 22 Condemned. Make enquiries as
to special circumstances attaching to any of them
which would warrant His Excellency exercising
mercy. Report at once, and get execution-orders
prepared as far as may be in blank for, say,
eight.”
“Yes, sir!”
Then the First Assistant Clerk went into
his room and delegated his assistant to wait
upon the Principal Superintendent of Con-
victs, and the Sheriff, and the Clerk of
Arraigns, with the view to procuring the
information necessary or desirable to be placed
before His Excellency the Governor-in-Council,
in order that that Providence in miniature might
decide which of the twenty-two were so fit for
Heaven that they could not longer be allowed to
pollute earth. And the assistant of the First-
Assistant listened deferentially, and made up his
mind that as they—the Executive—would not
hang more than eight or nine, he Would bother
his head about that number on the list and no
more. What was the use of looking up the
records of twp-and-twenty, when the work of
investigation as regarded two-thirds of that
number was foredoomed to be wasted? So he
reasoned and so he acted.
His assistant was a convict-writer. “Jervis,”
he ordered, writing out a pass as he spoke, which
would permit Convict-writer Jervis to pass through
the streets on public business ; “Jervis, goto
the Prin.-Sup.’s office and get the records of some
of the fellows condemned yesterday. You’ve got
the list there?”
“Yes sir. All, sir?”
“ No, take a dozen—no, eight will do !”
“ First eight, middle eight, or last eight, Mr.
Flippe?”
“ Oh," give ’em all an even chance. Throw the
ivories ! If you take first eight, the pris’ners in
the last case on the list won’t have a show at all.
You’ve got the pips there ? ”
Occasionally, Mr, Assistant-Clerk Flippe had
felt that the System subjected him to a severe
strain during office-hours, and he had recreated
himself after those times of stress by a judicious
handling of the dice-box. There was always the
c nvict-writer to play against, even supposing
that a brother free-clerk was indisposed to risk a
damn or a ring-dollar on the hazard of the die.
And’then, too, the dice-box and its cubes saved,
at other times, unnecessary mental wear and tear,
Suppose, for instance, some hundred or two appli-
cations for tickets-of-leave, duly authorised and re-
commended, were received during a single month,
it would be impossible to submit so large anumber
in one batch to His Excellency. A process of
selection would be indispensable, and nothing
would be easier than to throw the dice to decide
which applications should go in this month, and
which next month, unless, indeed, the simple plan
of throwing the names into a hat and drawing the
successful Ipplicants therefrom was resorted to.
But the dice box had an advantage over the
simple ballot, for the excitement which follows
upon the determination of competing chances is
greater than the excitement of mere selection,
As a means of deciding which good-conduct
transports were entitled to remission of servitude,
the use of the pipped “ivories ” was thus, you will
agree, unquestionably superior to anv other device,
It was so impartial. And this being so, what better
method could be adopted in deciding which of the
Condemned should live, and which should die?
Fate herself must use the dice in allotting their
destinies to human creatures, and there could be,
therefore, no valid objection to the lesser Fates of
the System employing the same machinery. But,
objection or no objection, it was all One to
Assistant-Clerk Flippe and his subordinate
Convict-Writer Jervis.
# # .■
They began.
Jervis took the slip on which was inscribed the
names of the Condemned, and pencilled figures 1
to 22 against the names, and then repeated the
same progression of numbers on ahother sheet,
“ Ready, sir!” he informed Mr. FHppe, And
Mr. Jervis twirled the dice-box, and flung the
paltry bits of bone out on his table. One of them
rolled on to a sheet of blue paper which had been
carelessly thrown down to wait registration and
filing. It was the Sheriff’s report of the previous
month’s executions which thus received the cube,
Flippe noticed the circumstance, and laughed.
“ That’s bad for No. 1, surely! He’s eager to
get on the coffin-list, isn’t he, Jervis ? NoW, how
many for him ?” He counted the three dice,
“ Six—five—two—total, thirteen I That one on
the coffin-list did him, Jervis ! It didn’t keep tip
the average for him. Put thirteen down to No. 1,
Jervis. Who is he?”
“ Moore, sir. ■’
“ Then I’ll sweat Moore Will be scragged,
Jervis !” _
Oh, thirteen’s pretty fair, Mr. Flippe. He’s
not half a bad chance.”
“I’ll back my judgment, Jervis—a fig of
tobacco against half-a--day’s work that Moore’s
left in?”
“Make it two figs, Mr. Flippe, and I don’t
mind. I’ll have to do the work at night if I lose,
and two figs are little enough for running the risk
of being nabbed by the watch before I get to the
barracks.”
“All right—it’s a Wager. Now, let’s see what
luck No. 2 has.”
No. 2 had worse. Flippe threw for him—Keyte
—a miserable, contemptible nine.
“Ha, ha, Mr. Flippe I” laughed the exultant
writer, who felt himself already in mastication of
two figs of succulent negrohead, “ ha, ha, Moore’s
chance is improving !”
“Jervis, you ass, you forget there are twenty to
go yet. It’ll be strange if I can't throw twelve
out of twenty above thirteen —deuced strange,
considering my usual luck with the beauties.” He
threw a third time, and confirmed his judgment
by turning up three sixes—a feat which he
repeated on the fourth throw,

With a lighter heart, for he did not enjoy the
rospect of doing Flippe’s work after hours, Jervis
noted No. 20’s eleven points. Connor was No. 20
___ a f e llow-offender with Moore. As he recollected
that fact, Convict-writer Jervis trusted the Scrip-
ture would be realised in this case—that of two
men one should be taken and the other eft.
11 “jfa 21!” cried the arbiter of life and death,
«No. 21! What say for No. 21. Treble sixes
again, by George! Lord, Jervis, if I were only
throwing Flicker, of the Lands, for a holey-dollar
on the best five easts out of seven and a dump on
the pip, wouldn’t I skin him ! Treble sixes, five
times out of twenty-one ! Whose neck did that
gave v* s
‘‘ Harris, sir— that black Norfolker, sent down
from the Island for maiming an overseer—a
desperate devil, sir.”
“ Ah, I’m sorry for that. But we must play
fair— can’t take his chance away and give it to
another. Now, here goes for the last —No. 22 !
jlowdoes the list stand, though, Jervis?”
The convict-writer, with knitted brows, bent
over his paper to count the several totals.
“Five 18’s, two 17’s, one 16, two 15’s, three
j4* g ”
“ That’s thirteen —they’re as good as reprieved,”
interrupted Mr. Flippe.
“One with 13 ”
“ That’s the nag which carries your money,
Jervis—Moore. Goon.”
“ One each 12, 11, 10, 9 and 6, and two with 7.”
“ That makes 21. Now for No. 22. Who’s
No. 22, Jervis ?”
“Fleming, the bushranger, sir.”
“ Then it’s Fleming and your man, Moore, for
it. Here goes !” The dice clicked in the twirling
of the box, and were cast out glibly.
“Thirteen, as I live ! Double fives and a tray !
They’re running neck and neck, Jervis ! ”
“ And made a dead-heat, Mr. Flippe ! ” The
Convict- Writer applauded with as hideous a joke.
* * #
Neck and neck, and a dead-heat it was, on
the first running, but in the throw-off (for as
eight were to be hanged, it was useless bothering
to send nine names in on the list) the last com-
petitor won.
“Now, Mr. Flippe ! ” Jervis humorously
pleaded, as Flippe intimated he was about to
throw on behalf of Fleming, the robber-under-
arms. “ Now, Mr. Flippe, don’t cast heavy this
time! Give mo a chance ! Those extra returns
of yours are very complicated, sir ! ”
“ I know they are, and that’s the reason I am
going to throw large if I can ! There ! ” The cubes
faced to the tune of seventeen.
Flippe sent his loud merriment into the adjacent
rooms. “You’ve as good as lost, Jervis! And
just to show you there’s no trick, Jervis, throw for
Moore yourself! ”
Jervis grasped the box, shook it, and sprawled
the dice eagerly. Perhaps he may have given a
thought to Moore’s fate, perhaps his thoughts
were intent only on evading the task of preparing
those troublesome returns. We cannot say.
All we know is that he threw seven.
* # #
That was the manner of choosing which
Eight should be consigned to Paradise. As soon
as the choice was made, Convict-writer Jervis was
sent out (on pass) to get an assortment of records;
and as soon as he obtained them, he prepared a
list of “suggested commutations,” which included
14 names, and transmitted it to Mr, Assistant-
Clerk Flippe. And as soon as Mr. Flippe re-
ceived it, he initialled it and passed it to the First
Assistant Clerk; and the First Assistant Clerk
initialled it and filliped it forward yet another
stage into the hands of Mr. Under-Secretary
Harrington ; and then the Colonial Secretary got
it, looked to see if it bore the initials of Mr.
Under-Secretary, and being satisfied upon that
point, put his initials upon it, and delivered it to
the Clerk of the Executive Council for presenta-
tioir to the Governor-in-Council. And when the
Governor-in-Council had done with it, there re-
mained but the filling-in of the death-warrants,
and the trifling little ceremony on the scaffold.
Now, Father Therry had grown wise with a
grim, ghastly wisdom. He had learnt that fight-
ing with men of the System was next to useless,
and that if he complained against subordinate
officialdom and were so far successful in his com-
plaining as to obtain the supercession of the
offenders, he would not succeed in permanently
improving the quality of the administration. One
subordinate official was just as indifferent as
another to considerations of propriety ; and if A
were reprimanded or removed from office, B, his
successor, would carefully plant his feet in A’s
footmarks confident that the spirit of reform had
exhausted itself for the time. Consequently,
Father Therry did not waste time in reporting to
the head authorities the way in which the death-
list was made up. That was the sort of crude
thing which he attempted in the Early Twenties.
Now, this epoch being that of the Early Thirties,
he was wiser. After all, this method of selection
was, he reflected, likely to be as just as any
other which the System would adopt. The
ballot for reprieves had old-time custom to
sanction it, and it was no more a lottery than
sticking a pin blindfold into the list of names,
which was the pretty method that had commended
itself to the graceful fancy of Mr. Secretary Goul-
burn when the “ capital ” report from the Sessions
had been inconveniently crowded. So he held his
peace, but prayed the more fervently for the early
downfall of the System and for the souls of the
Unreprieved.
* * *
The dice-box and the Governor’s preroga-
tive had taken the fourteen ; and the Eight who
had been left came out on the damp, drizzly
morning to offer themselves sacrifices unto the
gods of gloom.
There were Moore, Keyte, Kelly, and Connor 1 ,
condemned for a burglary and “putting in fear”
at a hut on a farm belonging to Gregory Blaxland,
Esquire, at Illawarra; there were Walley and
O’Hara, for burglary and “putting in fear” in
the dwelling of J. Raymond, Esquire, Postmaster
to the Territory ; there was Welsh, for cutting
and maiming at Norfolk Island—Harris was the
ruffian who perpetrated the assault, but Welsh
had stood by and had not interfered ; and there
was Crampton, for high way robbery with violence.
Eight—two Protestants (Crampton and Walley)
and the rest Catholics.
They came out into the lumber-yard, where
their coffins were before them. And though the
morning was sombre, they blinked at the light,
for each of the cells they had left clasped lovingly
in its recesses the darkness of the graves they
were so soon to occupy. Still, they would have
liked a brighter day.
The two welcomed Mr. Cowper with wan
smiles’. And most of the others, when their
irons were struck off them, grouped themselves
round the priest. But Moore, a lad of twenty or
thereabouts, stood, white-faced and shivering, by
himself.
A warder pushed him forward, gently. “There’s
the praste, Moore, honey ! An’ don’t yez lose the
blessid moments as is passin’, me bhoy !”
Moore dazedly listened, but didn’t move. _ The
numbing of death was already upon his spirit.
The anxious priest looked in his direction and
sought to draw him to join the now kneeling
group by a tender glance of sympathy. But the
lad either did not catch the look, or refused to
understand its import. He was a shapely youth,
with eyes that should have been bright blue in-
stead of bearing in them a dull glassiness, and
cheeks that should have been rounded and tinted
with flower-bloom instead of being drawn to sharp
angles, and pallid with the fear of the dark,
mysterious sleep symbolised by the coffin at his
feet. That he was swayed by terror of the direst
kind was apparent to the Father, who would have
gone to him, but that the others were clinging to
him.
“Moore!” at last, in a momentary silence,
which followed upon a response of the Condemned,
who were kneeling before him, he dared to say.
“ Moore, my son, come here !”
The call did not rouse the lad from his lethargy
but it drew Mr. Cowper’s attention to the diffi-
culty in which his brother cleric was placed. He
looked round ; his own charges, Crampton and
Walley, hardened in features though they were,
had softened into a reverence of demeanour ; they
might be left to themselves for a moment, he
thought, and he stepped toward the poor, stupi-
fied lad, with the purpose of persuading him to
attend the priest.
“ My dear lad,” he said, thrusting his right
arm from the surplice folds as he spoke to clasp
Moore’s hand, and putting his left hand, which
held his prayer-book, affectionately on the lad’s
shoulder, “ there’s no time to lose. Come to
Father Therry!”
The touch, more penetrative than the words,
stirred the drowsy faculties of the boy. A great
spasm, epileptic almost in its intensity, seized
him, and he writhed in the convulsions born of
his fear. The clergyman tightened his clasp, else
the condemned youth would have fallen —tightened
it, and drew the boy’s head close to his own
breast.
Just then the measured sound of tramping feet
overbore the murmur of the praying Condemned,
and by another door than that by which the eight
had emerged into the Lumber-Yard, the Sheriff
entered attended by four Javelin-men. Fearfully,
with the poignant agony which cannot express
itself, like the dog or the rabbit under the
vivisectionist’s haire, Moore looked round —and
He clutched the clergyman in a vyee-like grasp,
awoke, fully, to the imminence of the dread doom,
and then drew the surplice-skirts over his head.
But the linen could notmuffle theory with which he
followed his sudden movements: “Oh, sir, save
me, sir—l am innocent, I was never near Mr.
Blaxland’s —never, sir. Do save me, sir, for
Father Therry can’t, an’ I’ll join your Church,
sir ! I’ll be Purtestant, I will, sir! ”
• # « .
Cut to the heart as “Old Man” Therry
was by that piteous cry, he was tenderestto Moore
once the fatal procession was formed.
“Never mind us, Father!” said O’Hara.
“There’s that poor Moore, sor. Luk to him!”
And the others of his penitents motioned consent,
and freed him for that painfullesb of ministra-
tions.
The boy was being helped along the muddy
path which led to the gateway into the street by
two of the Javelin-men when the priest spoke to
him, with an accent of authority in his tones. It
hurt him to use it, but he thought it best to do so.
“ Moore, be quiet, lad ! Be quiet, I say ! ” ——
“ I’m innocent! I was not there !” moaned the
lad. “ I’ll not die !”
“ If you are innocent, you die a martyr, and
the greater glory yours, dear Moore ”
“ I’m innocent! I was not there 1” went on the
plaint of the Condemned.
“Ah, Moore, lad”
“ I’m innocent. I was not there!”
“ I believe you, Moore! The Blessed God
knows I do”
“You believe I’m innocent, and yet you—you
let me die ! You’re no priest! You’d have saved
me else !” The moaning cry changed horribly to
an imprecatory bitterness.
“You’re no priest! I’ll turn Protestant, I
will!”
# * *
Because the morning was dull, perhaps, or
because there had been several like ceremonies
during the previous month or two, the crowd was
not very numerous. Had it been, however, five-
fold in number, it would have been thrilled to its
farthest margin by the words spoken from the
scaffold.
“ I’m innocent! I was never there !” cried the
boy, as the Sheriff asked him what he had to say
before he was capped. “ Was I there, Keyte ?”
Through his capped lips, Keyte muttered a
monosyllable: “No!”
“Was I there, Connor?”
Connor was not yet capped. His “No !” rang
out clearly, unhesitatingly, gladly.
“Was I there, Kelly ?”
Kelly was capped. He either did not attempt
to speak or could not make himself heard. But
he moved his head from side to side, in ah unmis-
takable negative.
“ There, sir ; there, Mr. Sheriff, will you let me
be hanged now !”
For answer, the Sheriff lifted his eyebrows.
And the cap was over the turning, twisting head
in half a second, and the clench of an assistant-
executioner’s hands held the convulsed limbs
firmly.
The Sheriff glanced down the row of capped,
pinioned creatures. He gazed in the eyes of the
chief hangman who was waiting, and
“Stay, one word!” cried Father Therry. “I
now declare to you Mr. Sheriff, and to you, good
people, that I believe Moore to be innocent! God
and all His blessed saints receive you, Moore !”
The Sheriff turned down his thumb.*
PRICE WARUNG.
* The Rev. J. J. Therry communicated the facts on
which this story is based to the Rev. Dr. Dunne, V.G., of
St. Joseph’s, Hobart, Tasmania, by whom it was told to
the writer.

From an “elegy” by a new English
poet}—
(the wo6d is bare ;■ a river-mist is steeping
The trees that Winter’s Chill of life bereaves,
Only their stiffened boughs break silence, weeping
Over their fallen leaves.
In the early silver days of the Barrier,
two “ bushies ” cast anchor at a pub, a few miles
out of Silverton to go through the lambing-down
operation, One week sufficed to kill one, and
leave the other just alive. Things were done off-
handed in that shanty, and no time was lost
sending to town (some 14 miles) for a “box,”
which on arrival was found to be about a foot
short. Solemn council was held to bridge the
difficulty. Present (as the reporter says)— the
landlady, two of her friends, and deceased’s mate.
Business was gone into straight. “ Break ’is legs
and turnemup,” suggested the landlady; “don’t
see no other way.” This the landlady’s party
would have made the resolution of the meeting,
but for the blurted-out soliloquy of the mate—
“No ! damme if I'll see Tom’s legs broken.”
Then a long pause, which was broken by the
landlady addressing the question to the soliloquiser
—“What would yer do, then?” Tom’s mate had
not thottght of a way out of the trouble till the
query was put, but with a sudden, happy smile on
his boiled-looking countenance, he said—“ Cut his
head off and stack it in ’long with his legs !”
All parties joined in a discussion on this at once,
which Was only terminated by an appeal to a
thirsty soul who had just stepped in. “Wy!”
said he, “knock the tail o’ the blessed corfin out.”
And Tom now sleeps on the hillside with his feet
out in the cold.—P.N,

The Ancient Sailor-Man.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
According to a Melbourne paper, one of the
incidents at the wreck of the Fiji was the escape
of an ancient sailor, who came ashore grasping a
Bible. It had been given him by his mother, and
in three separate wrecks he had come ashore with
the treasured volume, everything else being lost.—
He was an ancient sailor with a dark, storra-
' beaten nose*
And he had a tattered Scripture that was hidden
in his clothes;
On the blackened rocks he sat and told the story
Of his Woes;
His mother gave the book to him in the joyous
days of old,
And often he had read it by a slush-lamp in the
hold,
When the frozen gale was shrieking and the
crested billows rolled.
And then, one day of gloom and wrack, the ship
fell overboard,
While in the wintry gloaming the frantic tempest
roared,
And the blast fell on the captain, and that officer
was floored:
Bdt this Saiibt seized his Scripture and started for
'the land ;
He reached it, damp and naked, with the Bible
in his hand,
While the other men were eaten by the prawns
along the strand.
He sailed again—the barque was loaded up with
kerosene,
And floating by some coral isles with hills of living
green,
The lightning struck it, and that vessel vanished
from the scene.
The starboard watch was shrivelled up and perished
in a heap,
The evil-minded second mate was frizzled in his
sleep,
And the steward seized the anchor and vanished
in the deep:
ilut this sailor-man arose, and o’er the blazing
bdlwarks he—
With all his whiskers flaming and his hair oil
fire -did flee,
And quenched his smouldering Bible in the silent,
emerald sea.
For months he lived alone upon an archipelago,
With his half-combusted Testament, and watched
the seasons go,
And the weary, glassy ocean in its ceaseless ebb
and flow.
He listened to the moaning winds and heard the
land-crab roar,
And watched the winkle as it walked upon the
ocean floor.
Till he was picked up by a ship bound for Aus-
tralia’s shore.
Its captain was a reckless man, and now and then
he cussed,
And sometimes, in his earlier days, had gone upon
the bust,
And the language of the crew would fill a Christian
with disgust.
So when the winds arose, and ghosts were shriek-
ing on the blast,
Hat sailot knew the voyage was the evil vessel’s
last,
And he took his Bible in his teeth and hung on to
the mast.
Next with a gruesome crash the noble vessel came
to grief,
And hung in fragments on the teeth of a black and
jagged reef,
And the sea was full of anchors, and jibbooms,
and kegs of beef.
But sundry natives lit a mighty bonfire on the
strand,
And presently they saw an aged sailor come to
land,
And put a sodden Scripture down to dry upon the
sand.
Then he sat down beside it with a sad, abstracted
look.
And musingly he chased the whelks from off the
sacred book
Till it was dry—and then be picked it up, and
took his hook.
TITUS SALT.
SHE PUT IT IN THE CUPBOARD.
What a weary thing it is to be ill, especially for a
long time. You are tired of advice, tired of try-
ing this, that, and the other, tired of fancying you
are a trifle better, tired of taking precautions
against getting worse. You are tired of pain, and
half believe the sooner you are under the ground
the better for you, and the more agreeable to your
friends.
Here’s a little story. A woman told it lately.
She says:
[Copy,]
“I, Mary Jones, of 3, Gal ton-street, Great
Howard-street, Liverpool, declare as follows :
“I have suffered all my life from weakness of
the stomach and sluggish liver. I was always
tired and languid, and often troubled with bilious
headache. My appetite was poor. I never had
desire for food, and what I did eat did not digest
properly. I had always a bad taste in my mouth,
and a slimy scum would cover my mouth and
teeth, and so bad was this that I was obliged to
rinse it away before eating. When I was in my
teens I was very weakly and would faint after
sitting down to my meals. I had trembling at
the heart, with a heavy weight at the chest, pain
at my side, and a strange giddy feeling would
come over me. When out walking in the street I
used to have to stand and rest for fear of falling.
I was also so weak that when going about my
work I often had to sit down and rest. At times
I had a bad cough and pain in my lungs. I saw
doctor after doctor, and was under medical treat-
ment for more than twelve years. One doctor
said I was suffering from an enlargement on the
liver, another said I had heart disease, a third
said my lungs were congested and that I could not
recover. Thinking I was now in a consumption,
I went to a consumptive hospital and was under
treatment there for some time. The doctors, after
sounding my chest, told me I was in a decline,
and that my left lung was congested. They gave
me cod-liver oil and other medicines, but I got no
better.
“ In July, 18£0, after a bad fainting, I became
so bad I had to take to my bed, and was attended
by three doctors for a month. The first doctor
who saw me said I could not recover. I had
intense pains in my stomach, and could get
nothing to pass my bowels. Various means were
tried and found useless. I could take liquid food
only, and was fast sinking, my case being con-
sidered hopeless by both the physician and the
nurse. At this time it was that my husband
heard through Mr. Parry, the chemist in Great
Howard-street, of a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and we were presented
with a book which described a case just like mine
having been cured by it. My husband procured
a bottle of this medicine and I began taking it.
After a few doses I passed a motion as black as
coal and experienced great relief, and shortly after
felt as if I wanted something to eat. When the
doctor called the next day he could see a marked
change in me and said, ‘Mrs. Jones, we have
given you the right medicine at last.’ He thought
it was his last bottle that had worked the change,
whereas the nurse had put the medicine in the
cupboard untouched. 1 went on with Mother
Setgel’s Syrup, and to the delight of my husband,
father, and nurse I never looked behind me. All
the pain at the heart and chest gradually left me,
and in a fortnight I was strong enough to be
removed to the seaside, and have ever since been
in good health,
“I never felt so w r ell in my life as Ido now.
All my friends consider the cure as a miraculous
one, and I wish others to know by what means
my life was saved. lam willing that Messrs. A.
J. White, Limited, shall make whatever use they
may think fit of this statement, and I hereby
authorise them to do so.
“Dated this 3rd April, 1891.
“(Signed) Mrs. Mary Jones.”
There was nothing “miraculous” about this
lady’s recovery, although it may have been, and
indeed it was, remarkable. But it was all along
the straight lines of nature. She had suffered all
her life from indigestion and dyspepsia, and her
other ailments arose from that, and that alone.
She could not digest her food, and the whole sys-
tem was perishing from the poison in the stomach
and from lack of nutrition. Mother Siegel’s
Syrup did its one work : it cleansed away the cor-
rupted matter and set the digestive organs (the
liver, stomach, and bowels) in natural operation,
and immediate improvement and final recovery
followed as necessary consequences.
Especially should the reader notice the close
resemblance between the symptoms of dyspepsia
and those of consumption, through which most
unhappy mistakes are constantly made. Never
conclude you have consumption until you are sure
it isn’t dyspepsia. The chances are that the
results will be the same as in Mrs. Jones’ case.

William Wilson, an octogenarian sun-
downer, camped for the last time near Goondi-
windi on the lower side of a fire he had built tip
against a huge log. While the old man slept, the
log, having burnt away its under part, rolled over
and crushed him to death. Only Australia could
produce a swagman roaming the bush at 81 years
of age.

Australian Cruelty.
In a recent cablegram the Australian temperament
is branded with “cruelty” on the authority of
-r rancis Adams.
_ tendency to cruelty is derived from our
English parents, who, among other things, said,
It will cure you ” because the nearest farmer
raked us aft with coarse salt from a blunderbuss,
as we struggled over his fence laden with green
apples.
Who shut us up in a dark room with the devil,
because we d d the eyes of the parson, for
mangling our baby toes as he crumpled our tiny
paw after morning service !
Who threatened to transfer us to the “Vernon ”
and to have us put in irons because we asked the
Lord in a prayerful whisper to note how we would
er w ° en we f> rew U P to man’s estate?
Who opened the oven-door and thrust us in
because we held the kittens near the fire to make
em squeak ?
Who tied us to the bed-post and menaced us
with a red-hot poker, because we struck matches
on the cot, in which that blessed cherub baby-
sister was slumbering ?
Who stuck a skewer into our leg, because we
angled the neighbour’s pet game-cock with a
large bread crumb, a bent pin, and six feet of blue
twine ?
Wliorwhipped us because we hitched an un-
healthy male goat to a go-cart and knocked the
“ stuffin ” out of him because he wouldn’t pull.
Who threatened to hang us by the neck till
dead, and actually suspended us by the body from
a beam because we tied a piece of cord across the
garden path, and then told our infant brother to
run down hard, which he did and spilt himself on
the gravel, making his flat nose flatter and
disfiguring his noble baby brow ?
Who threw our fireworks (representing four
months’ frugality) into the wash-tub because we
fired a rocket from the cook’s ankles ?
Who found a loose tooth in our head and
yanked it out with crochet-cotton, because we
fractured the butcher boy’s tooth with our
shanghai ?
Who held our nose and tipped half-a-bottle of
castor oil into us because we swallowed a taw ?
Who put us on dry bread and water for a day
because we gave the monkey at the Zoo a pebble
tied up in a few sheets of brown paper?
Who chained us by the leg to the dog-kennel
because we tied our new “ play pannikin ” and a
jam-tin to the poodle’s tail ?
Who grinned when we were chained because the
next door boy got upon the fence, leered at us,
defied us, thrust out his tongue laying it flat along
his chin and then curling it upwards to the tip
of his nose, closing one eye as he did so, put his
fingers to his nose, yelled—“ Here’s a lark ! Rats !
Rats! Sool ’em, Mungo;’’spat on a chip, flung
it away and shrieked, “Fetch it; ” ate an orange,
“ shied ” the peel at us, peppered us with the pips
through a pea-shooter, and cheeked us beyond en-
durance ?
Who christened us “ Sikes ” when we knocked
the next door boy off the fence with a shin-bone
and laid him up for five weeks with brain fever?
Who banished us to a boarding-school because
we fired at the stable-door with our air-gun and
drove a pellet through the stove-pipe hat of the
governor, who was inside the stable inspecting
our new pony ?
Who firstly and lastly recognised in us the dis-
position of our mother’s brother, and the nature
of our father’s mother ?
J. M.
You may certainly state that no one but a crank,
Would burgle a safe ’stead of opening a bank ;

The men who saw Mayfcown in the heyday
of its prosperity would not now know the place.
With empty dwellings, deserted streets, and its
population of disease-stricken niggers and opium-
smoking Chows, the town presents a true example
of the yellow agony’s inevitable results. When
the news of the great Palmer Eldorado reached
China, the Chows flocked to Cooktown in scores
and hundreds till, during the height of the rush,
there were between 18,000 and 20,000 of them on
the field. Most of this horde have returned to
China, many have died, while others, worse luck
remained, and are now merchants, butchers, bakers
and hotelkeepers in and about Maytowu—in fact,
Maytown is nothing but a small Canton. There
is now not an English storekeeper or baker in the
place.
You can obtain from B. Mountcastle & Sons, of
Albert-street, Brisbane, goods of a similar class to
th it retailed by the leading West-end hosiers.

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Reaper, rested by refrain,
Re-grasped his sickle keen ;
Said he—“ I seek the bearded grain,
Not the flowers that grow between.”
Years syne, two boys at school did fag—
One bold, one shy and meagre ; °
Their mates nicknamed the first “ Jack >»
The second, “ Betsy Eagar.” ' n ’
Jack grew a foremost man of mark,
A politician fervent;
While Betsy sought the Gov’ment ark—
Became a Civil Servant.
' * * • •
For years the Reaper paid no heed
To either, true or fickle ;
But let them ripen, run to seed,
Ere sharp’ning up his sickle.
He watched the western shadows creep,
Each hour-glass spend its measure ;
Then reaped in Jack with sudden sweep
Took Betsy at his leisure.
Their “souls march on,” their bodies rot
Beneath the graveyard’s heather;
Men wonder what nicknames they’ve got
Now they’re once more together.
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Dear Bulletin,— Having lived 13 years in
N.S.W., I can endorse all Christie Murray’s' assert inns
about the native-born Australian. 3 here is one thinir
Mr. Murray forgot to mention: the dishonesty of the
native-born Australian. With few exceptions here and
there they are mostly dishonest. There is another feaUne
in the native-born Australian I wish to mention : ho can
absorb any amount of false flattery and puffery, but a
word of honest truth sends him raving mad. — Balm aiv
[Next! Ed. B.] AW '
The latest natural-history yarn is told by
selector Le Mortemore, of Tinana Creek (Q.). He
has captured a tree-climbing pig, weighing about
1 cwt., and furnished with “ bristly brown fur ”
small black spots, pig’s snout and ears, front teeth
like a rat, large canines and powerful grinders •
fore-feet with hook-like claws, hind-feet with two
hook-claws each ; thick, prehensile tail a foot
long, generally carried in a Flemish coil ; a pouch
in which it carries its surplus food while “on the
wallaby.” Le Mortemore killed and ate this
“pig,” and avers that the flesh is excellent, and
tastes like veal and-ham pie. The animal climbs
trees and hangs cn to the boughs by its tail, while
it gathers grub. Le Mortemore is going to send
the skin to the Maryborough Exhibition, (Q.), and
intends to capture some live specimens and breed
from them. One theory lias been started that the
animal is a hybrid, the progeny of the marriage of
the common pig with the tree-climbing kangaroo.
Another theory is feasible : that the tale of Le
Mortemore is like the tail of the pig—thick, pre-
hensile and a foot long, and carried in a Flemish
coil.
Thus a correspondent ;—
“Price Warung’s” bookworm reminds me of a some-
what similar character, not unknown to Sydney people a
quarter of a century ago. Robert Vance had been a
clerk in the N.S.W. Treasury in the days of the old
Regime, and was enjoying a pension, that is, he enjoyed
it in his own fashion. He owned a terrace of three or
four houses in Sussex-street near Liverpool-street, in one
of which he lived alone, a miser’s life, in the midst of
filth. In an upper room, which was used for all domestic
purposes, he had a splendid library of standard authors
in elegant bindings stowed away in all manner of boxes
and other receptacles. He wished the writer to find him
a customer for the lot, but on visiting the place the odour
was so overpowering that a hasty retreat was beaten.
He was recommended to offer his library to Sir
Redmond Barry, but with what result is not
known ; certain it is that on his death the
books had disappeared. The old fellow dressed in a
most singular manner, an old watch coat down to his
shoes, an old cotton handkerchief round his neck, and the
most ancient and oddly-shaped of stove-pipe hats. Not a
hit of white was v isible, and people used to say that the
hat, the coat, the shoes and the cotton “ wipe ” were the
only clothes he wore. He was supposed to be without
relatives, but when his death was announced a nephew
substantiated his claim, and succeeded to the property.
Then a claimant appeared in the person of a now M.L.C.,
who sued for some hundreds of pounds sterling, which
he claimed to have advanced to the old man. The
accounts w ere kept in what is known as a “ penny pass-
book,” and were disputed by the heir-at-law. A Supreme
Court jury, however, found for plaintiff. The old house,
in Sussex-street, was demolished, and the land now lies
idle.
Good old Ambrose Bierce in the S.F.
Examiner :—
I assume that this paper’s account of Mrs. Lucy Pratt
submitting to the removal from her body of 45 square
inches of skin to patch up a railway mail clerk scalded
in a collision is true. I assume that it is true that she did
this in order.to obtain a reward of one hundred dollars
offered by him and that she needed the money for sup-
port of herself and her children. Well, I have not the
gratification of approving the transaction. I do not
think the life of any male of our species worth 45 square
inches of skin taken from any part of a good woman’s
body. It is to he hoped the brave woman will recover,
and that the selfish man (whose name is Daggett) will
live or die, just as ho would have done without the patch-
ing. If men are ever too mean to die he will be spared
to us until he experiences a change of heart. An
honourable man would have suffered a thousand deaths
rather than accept his life on such terms as the mutila-
tion of a good woman; her assent cuts no figure
in the matter. On learning that she was willing
and needy, Mr. Daggett should have given her
the money and died like a gentleman. This
peeling a woman to re-cover a man will not do—
emii ently not. There is law, too, for its suppression. If
the three physicians—one a woman who seem to have
been concerned in the Prat,I-Daggett affair have not
subjected themselves to indictment, I’m a crop of
barley! It would he very funny to see my good and
clever friend Dr. Chismore playing at checkers with his
nose because he could not curb the lust for skin-grafting
nor refrain from joining together what God had put
asunder. Nay, he was not required to curb, but only
regulate: to the right and sufficient gratification of his
ambition he might have repaired the Daggett with a
broad slab of congenial dog. Or he could have half-soled
the fellow with cow, and then the chap would have “ had
good leather in him.” But the skinning of women for
any purpose whatever is not in harmony with the spirit
of the law. Consent be jamboozlcd I A woman’s skin ia
inalienable ; she has no right to shed it. She is not that
kind of snake.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong, ‘
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugblmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left l.ung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectoratingevery day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter S.mvtiie, J.p.
